RATING: 7.5
A non-rondel game from the rondel master. This time he cleverly and seamlessly slipped the rondel feel into a hand of action cards with Ancient Rome exploration and building as the backdrop. 

Gameplay involves selecting an action card and executing it each turn, with one if the actions being to get your hand of cards back again. Everyone starts with the same hand but during the course of the game your deck will grow and change. The rules and gameplay are relatively simple but the strategy and decisions are not. The goal is to get the most points and these are obtained through multipliers on the cards and payout similar to Navegador. 

The actions are as follows: 1) move (optional) and build in a city/region (this is a coveted action as presence in regions/built cities key off of many of the point multipliers), 2) purchase new cards (the more cards the more options and the more points), 3) generate resources from a region or take money, 4) copy an action played by another player, 5) buy and/or sell resources which is very important for cash generation and resource flexibility, and 6) retrieve your cards, while generating a small amount of cash and having an opportunity for generating more colonists. There are more of the same, different, and similar cards that you can acquire through option 2.

The game goes into end phase when either the last card has been purchased or when a player has built his last house (these are built in cities). Points are scored at the end, are scored for several different categories, and can be huge IF you focus on meshing your card collection with your actions taken (example creating a lot if new colonists and purchasing cards with colonist cards- the temptation is simply to card grab).

The game board is double-sided for different number of players and the game seems to scale well for 3-5 players (I have not tried 2er yet). The components are excellent as always for a Gerdts game. This is a very solid, professional design.

Own the [boardgame=165023]Britannia & Germania[/boardgame], [boardgame=181084]Salsa[/boardgame],
 [boardgame=208364]Galia/Corsica[/boardgame], and [boardgame=232917]Aegyptus /Creta[/boardgame] expansions as well as the [boardgame=209574]Forum cards[/boardgame] promo.

RATING: 8
12+ plays / 2,3,4,5 players (2h-2.5h)

LIKE: Good decision tree, between obtaining cards for actions or points. Some fun elements of engine building. Smaller maps are better as they create more tension for building (Italy was good w/3 & 4). Variable setup. Easy to learn because your actions are on the cards.

DISLIKE: Dry. Difficult to get to the table, until people are familiar with how enjoyable the game is.

OVERALL: You really have to balance your resource engine, but it seems there aren't any bad moves, only moves that are more efficient, so a very forgiving game. Tighter maps lead to better interaction. Point deduction on the rating because the game has a dry theme, but otherwise a fun game.

RATING: 8
2016-02-25
Concordia is an excellent game in which the key is to phase your own actions so that you are always one step ahead of other players' but in sync with yours.  The game revolves around constructive actions that all inevitably take the game closer to its finish. There are no negative actions, which creates an atmosphere that can be enjoyed by almost anyone. Player interaction is based on blocking, leeching and being the first to do something. And the game is very interactive, it just is not of the "In Your Face" type. 

After the game you have a feeling that you actually accomplished something since, you can see everything you bought right in front of your eyes. And you can achieve quite a bit, which is refreshing after all the games with very tight economy. The dramatic arc has a nice curve and the game picks up some speed towards the end in an predictable manner. 

I have to admit that the theme is rather generic and the game does not look exciting at all. If you can get past this, Concordia offers an enjoyable experience in hand and resource management. It is not a revolutionary game in any way, or a game that will be found in every home for that matter, but it simply works like a charm.

RATING: 8
An excellent game -- Mr. Gerdts has done it again!  This time his infamous rondel is found in how you play your hand of cards.  It's interesting how the cards drive the game.  You want to produce goods to sell and/or spend goods to get more cards to score more points but you need to keep with the produce/consume to do anything at all.

I want to learn more about the strategies with this game.  I'm still very much at a loss as to optimal paths.  But then so much depends on which cards you get and where the goods markers are on the map...

There's definitely a potential for the rating (currently an 8) to go up.  Probably tops out at a 9 for me.  Great game but not quite making my top 10 (so to speak).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Mac Gerdts is my favorite game designer. His games always surprise me for being so easy to teach and play and yet so deep and rich in strategy and tactics. Concordia is no exception.

Artwork and components match the usual good quality of PD-Verlag's products. The graphic design might not be as crispy as, say, Asmodee's games but it's always functional, easy to the eye and esthetically pleasing. I actually even kinda like the box art and I don't understand why people are hating so much on it. On the mechanisms front instead, while Concordia abandons the rondel, its card-play mechanism doesn't move very much away from the smaller actions and low downtime format that is Mac Gerdts' signature. I like this a lot about his games, but I think that in Concordia the typical snappiness of turns is not as strong and that there's more downtime in this game compared to his other titles.

On the game-play side of things Concordia falls again in some of Gerdts' typical characteristic. The game is less about trying out different strategies and more about implementing those strategy better and more efficiently than the other players. On one hand this means less variety of experiences and on the other one it provides you with a great challenge every time. Concordia is really an "easy to learn, hard to master" kind of game. Another strong point in its favor as far as I'm concerned!

Yet, strangely enough, even if Concordia is the one game that doesn't have the rondel and therefore should be the most diverse of its titles, somehow it's the one that gives me the feel of being too similar to his other works. I maybe see to much of Navegador in it. It could also be that I played it last among his other games. Nonetheless this brings my enjoyment of Concordia a little bit down. Fortunately the Salsa expansion added quite a bit of new content to the base game, increasing its variety and spicing things up by giving players access to cool special powers. I like the game more with this expansion.

To sum up Concordia has all the great characteristics of a Gerdts' design that I enjoy so much. Quick turns, tough decision, great depth. Just I don't enjoy it as much as Imperial or Antike, because it feels just a tiny bit derivative. Still good game though, especially if you include the expansion... and let's face it: Mac Gerdts never truly misses!

Rating without the expansion: 7

Impressions after one, 3p play: depth is not emergent, relying instead more on a convoluted set of cards and trading economy. Taxing by making you figure out how to line up 27 different things just-right to make them work, and I don't think a single coin negates the early-mover advantage (at least on the map I played, where the starting locations were basically in a corner). Scoring coming entirely from card-buying things with tiny images on the other end of the table... also not great design, IMO.

Would grudgingly play again, but probably at no more than 4p, and preferably 3, and have many better games of this depth and length to choose from. But it's so blah for something in the top 50; at times, it felt a bit more like doing inventory management than having fun. 

I'm sure if I could actually be bothered to learn all the stupid cards and commit to memory all the exchanges and building costs, I'd like it a little more. But probably only a little.

RATING: 8.5
[i]2017/05/15 - 2 plays 2-4 players[/i]

This game is a great example of deep strategy from a very simple ruleset. The game has a good progression from early-mid-end game, and can be quite tense at any stage, provided one uses the appropriate map for each player count (Corsica is great for 2p, for instance).

Although I love this game, it has some caveats to mind : the game can be a bit dry for some players. I also lasts surprisingly long for its apparent simplicity. The scoring at the end can also be a turn off for some players, since it's pretty opaque, at least for inexperienced players.

RATING: 8.75
The theme is definitely there, albeit not heavily; and the beautiful art certainly adds to the themeing. It just feels "Roman", whatever that means! But to say it's "dripping with theme" would, admittedly, be like saying the moon is covered with lush kelp forests.

As for Concordia being a deck-builder, it's far more of a deck-management game, and does that very well. Gone is Herr Gerdt's rondel, of which I am not at all a fan. Curiously, the playing of cards is remarkably similar to Lewis&Clark; yet in spite of that, the two games are utterly different!

It is a strategic game with a strong chaser of tactics, that keeps the players on their toes and ensures a great deal of replayability. There is an element of multi-player solitaire in Concordia, but really, that's only because no one can attack you directly. Your opponents are actually very annoying, although that it is due almost exclusively to the "Hey! You took my spot/card!" phenomenon. 

Negatives? Only two, really. Since you can't really see what kind of multiplier cards your opponents have, there can be an element of guess-work as to how they're doing. And by the way: if it is your first game, you simply MUST play it with the intermediate-scoring rule! That helps a lot, since it painfully reveals the reality of how scoring works in the game. Without it, you will be in for some nasty surprises at game-end. But, if each player has played Concordia at least once, there's really no need for it.

The second negative is that Concordia is just a smidgeon too long for what it offers you. Other than that, it is a wonderfully deep game with a mere four pages of rules!

RATING: 8
Concordia isn't really all that complex or groundbreaking, but I find it to be a particularly pleasant Euro. I think it's neat that Gerdts managed to basically design another rondel game without a rondel. The card play gives you a nice sense of pacing with your actions.

The game is at its weakest when players pay very little  attention to what the others are doing. It's at its strongest when players pay attention to the VP goals others are going for and fight over getting relevant cards from the queue for screwage and VP gains.

Basically, the game is interesting when it's a struggle over competing VP goals with a little bit of "hate drafting" of what the other players want, and it's a bit boring if played as the dreaded "multiplayer solitaire." But it's easy to drift into the MP solitaire mode because of how intuitively the game invites you to just putz around building up your little economic industry on the board  w/o focusing on the VP battle. Plus, more players (it goes up to 5) adds more chaos and makes it more difficult to engage with what others are doing.

RATING: 4
Concordia may have dispensed with the rondel, yet it has Mac Gerdts' signature all over it. There are still the single action player turns thanks to action cards.  Concordia is also a well-developed game with no sharp edges or clunky mechanisms.  Like Navegador it feels a highly abstracted design that happens to be played out on a map.  

The innovation this time is handbuilding, picking up cards from a face-up row of reducing cost.  Cards all feature actions, but also a God affiliation that provides a multiplier for vps related to various things.

Concordia feels rather slow to get going - building up from almost nothing in incremental steps - and very slow to end.  It's not helped by a feeling of same old, same old collecting resources and converting them into victory points.

There is nothing exceptional about the artwork, but it is fairly pleasing to the eye.  Production quality is good.

RATING: 1
Like a loooong slog through thigh deep mud, only to arrive just as someone beats you to it. Which wouldn't be so bad if it didn't take several turns to set up for every move. Then you can try something else if you have the correct resources, or go on with your plan if you have the money to pay double. Cards cannot be counted on as by the time your turn rolls around it may be a completely new set and not ones you can afford or even use. Locations may favor the start players and that may or may not be mitigated by the "double prizes" for the last player. Almost no way to plan ahead. RATING: 7
This is a solid, intelligently designed euro that offers many respectable paths to victory.  The card play and deck building, with dual use aspects to the cards (card effect and end game VP multiplier) feels interesting to think about, and is perhaps the most "originalish" aspect to the game. (As an aside, I prefer the dual use aspect of the cards in Snowdonia - effect and VP win conditions.) 

Unfortunately, for my tastes, the rest of the game, the overall feel of the game, feels tired, like an essentially themeless placement of wooden houses around a map of europe, which after the hundredth time of doing this sort of thing in a euro game, I find boring.  And, of course, there is resource planning, and resource conversion, in order to place these houses on a map of europe or buy cards that help me. Yawn, boring! Gameplay is also a bit too solitary for my tastes.  Are we actually interacting here?  Barely. 

I greatly respect the intelligence and integration of the design mechanics in the game; they probably deserve an 8.  This is a masterful standard euro mechanic design.  But the tired, (let's be honest) themeless, solitaire gameplay in Concordia knocks this game down to a 7.  I barely want to play Concordia again, so that means I should probably give it a 6.  But, I am holding out hope that the second play will brighten my perspective on this game, so gonna give it a 7.

RATING: 7
The rules of this game are simple and elegant, but the decisions to be made are quite complex. The card play mechanic is great and requires a lot of strategizing and timing. Because of the elegance of the rules, it is easy to play. Thematically the game is very dry and almost totally abstract, but it has nice components and a beautiful map. Some complaints: 1) Even the Italy-map is too sparse with two players, so there is no confict at all in terms of map play; 2) It would be more fun if there were more thematic immersion; 3) The end game scoring is so difficult to predict that it takes away from strategy and interaction as it's almost impossible to predict your opponents' scores. It is a nice and elegant game, but the cleanliness of mechanics and play comes with the expense of immersion. The designer improved the design in every way in Transatlantic.

Fun factor: 3/5
Strategy vs. luck: 4/5
Mechanics/gameplay tied to the theme: 2/5
Component/art quality: 3/5 (components are 5, but the art is little bland)
Replayability: 4/5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.7
An easy to teach, hard to master euro with really good, elegant and intertwined mechanics that will let you pursue many different paths to victory, but requires surgical precision to juggle its many facets successfully.

The game consists of players taking turns playing exactly one card. You typically have between 3 and 9 distinct cards available to play each turn. Some have one simple effect while others further require you to make more decisions. But one thing remains constant: each decision is hard and matters.

You need to carefully develop your board and get the cards that will let you translate that tempo into victory points. If you get too greedy with the board or too greedy with the cards you will lose. You should balance getting diverse resources and money. If you get too greedy and neglect that cheap brick and grain it will come back and haunt you later. If you don't sell excess goods you will run out of space and money. If you don't balance resources and money you won't be able to expand through the map or get victory point cards.

One neutral, or controversial point, is the lone end of game scoring round. It is really hard to keep track of how well or bad players are doing, so first time players will feel lost in that regard. It is also harder to prioritize which player to block, bully or leave alone (which can be both good and bad). But it also good in that bad players won't feel like they are facing an insurmountable wall (at least until the end when they get kicked hard!). After a few playthroughs you will stop shooting in the dark and be more aware of how to win and strategize (and agonize) even more about the decisions you make. It will definitely shine with a regular playgroup and more experienced players, specially with the salsa expansion, that gives a wild resource to be used as if it came with the core game, optional starting variable powers, optional rewards for using the tribute card and a map that will take the total to 4, each best suited for each player count, ordered by city quantity.

Pros:
-Simple but deep game
-Cards as action selection and victory points is great.
-Resource storage and generation mechanics are excellent.
-All mechanics are great by themselves and together.
-It can provide a good experience for newbies and veterans.
-It has a very high replay value due to its randomized start, region bonus resource, available cards to buy, initial turn order giving distinct advantages, different feel at each player count (diplomat and prefect magnus become more important as player count increases and the maps become tighter), and not to mention optional variable starting powers and tribune rewards.

Cons:
-Box art and box font are bad, and a bit unappealing to people checking out your collection and deciding what to play, or in a store and deciding what to buy.
-You want to consider an extra cost for redoing the box cover.
-I think that the salsa expansion is critical for the game, so add its cost.
-The coin denomination of 2 is useful but redundant, and confusing because it is copper, which is typically the color of denomination 1 in most other games. The coin of 1 and 5 are silver, so I may have mistakenly paid the copper one as having a value of 1 instead of its printed 2.
-The maps should be a bit tighter in my opinion. Between 20 and 30 cities makes 2 player games feel to open and 5 player games too tight. I would like a map with 10 cities so 2 players can feel crowded.

I could keep talking about how good the mechanics all day, but the same can be said for how bad the box art is, or its font. It really scares away people and takes almost a point down from what could be a masterpiece. It is, at least mechanically. It just makes sense of how both combine to make this a sleeper home run.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Elegant, beautifully themed euro about trading in the Mediterranean, Roman-style. A handful of diverse but simple mechanics fit together smoothly and thematically. There are many routes to victory, and a delicious tension between expanding your trade empire (your goods-producing engine) and acquiring cards that dictate both how you'll score points and the flexibility of your actions. There's no direct conflict, but a great deal of interaction comes from the race for cities and rivalry over buying cards. The variable assignment of bonus tiles to provinces, variable location of cities, and order in which the cards come out of the draw deck means every new play is fresh.

RATING: 9.5
Such elegance...Concordia must be my favorite 'trading in the Mediterranean game', and my first experience with a Mac Gerdts design. The catch is, with expansion maps you can avoid the Mediterranean part. A load of strategy off playing a single card per turn, each one lets you produce goods, score money, travel the landscape, and lay down houses in cities. The variable setup is intuitive and wonderful, the board beautiful, the player warehouse boards give it a tactile edge, and I don't really have much of an issue with the cover art work as many seem to do. Scoring is surprisingly deep with the Personality cards often allowing you to double or further multiply, and there are other cards that can affect scoring. Just all around wonderful.

RATING: 10
This is probably my favourite game. It is so easy to teach to new players and the strategy in it is so quick to pick up that new players will find themselves scoring almost as much as experienced ones. To close the gap between new and experienced players the strategy you choose will reward you in points in the end, as cards also acts as points multipliers which most times rewards the actions on the card. 

I find it heavy enough to challenge me, but light enough to ensure that I can play more games afterwards, which is what is important to me.

RATING: 8.5
Lovely production, great to have two very different feeling maps available, which does wonders with preventing the game to feel stale before its time.
The game is rather hard to get into the very first time. No idea what makes a good strategy. You learn very swiftly, though, so this isn't a problem for long. It doesn't have as much of engine building as I'd expected, but also doesn't really need it. Feels like a mix of Power Grid and Navegador. Positioning is very important - as is always having a backup plan. Beyond that the game seems to be won mostly by making optimal use of your cards, and just eking out small advantages until they can snowball into the lead in a few settlements or cards. Both lovely and elegant.

RATING: 5
At last a non-rondel game from Mac Gerdts.  The rondel was a very clever innovation, which has subsequently been widely adopted and adapted.  Concordia is a solid design, but feels uninspired compared with the many new fresh ideas now being published.  It feels like a throwback to the nineties.  In the game we add bases and expand our network, acquire cards with VP multiples.  And then continues in the same vein until the end of the game.  The introduction of a nominal early scoring round in your first game (only I recall), which is largely wiped clean, was a very clever mechanism to make new players familiar with the scoring requirements.  But with a pile of other Spiel 2013 releases to get through, we called it a day after 90 minutes as we felt we had experience what the game had to 'offer' at that point.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
The Meaty, clever, Euro game you expected that takes ancient roman economics to the next level!

Well maybe not a whole level up, but the game is a great example of what this sort of game can bring to your table.  

Players start with a hand of cards (not a deck) which drive the action in the game.  With these cards players can colonize cities, produce goods, and add more cards to their hand.  Since players never draw from a deck, they never have to wait for a particular card to emerge and the timing is strictly under their control.

Components are nice (the map looks great), just try and look past that box art which is probably one of the worst in the business.

RATING: 8.5
Quite interesting hand-cards management game with a constant struggle to produce resources, sell them for money, in order to build houses that may give resources...
Each card gives you an ability, and one of them allows to recover the discarded.  You also get new cards, so that your hand increases.  
Scoring is a totally unknown during the game.  Each card is associated to a divinity, and each divinity scores for a different concept of the game.  So it is absolutely impossible to know what each player has.
It was not easy for me to "flow the game" in the first time, but looks interesting.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I love both versions of Imperial. Navegador is good, and I quite like every other rondel game designed by Mac Gerdts.
So I was a bit puzzled when I heard the "rondel master" would publish a game without his signature mechanic.

In Concordia, the rondel has been replaced with a deck of cards. Each player has an identical starting deck, but he can add different cards buying them from a display (à la Through the Ages), diversifying the options for later turns AND giving him special scoring bonuses for the end game. So when acquiring cards, one must always balance the need to add a given action OR a given scoring bonus.

During each turn, a player chooses one of the cards of his deck (all cards in the deck are in hand), plays if for the effect and discards it. Discarded cards can't be played again until a special card is played that allows to recover the discards.
This mechanic is clever and well implemented, and the game has lots of interesting decisions.

The goal of the game is building a commercial and influence network in the Roman world (on in Italy, if playing with just 2-3 players), sending colonists to settle different cities and establish commercial/production colonies.

Overall, the game is very very good. Possibly one of the best released at Essen Spiele 2013.

Initial tentative rating is an 8.

RATING: 7
A good management game, fluid and relatively accessible but not transcendent.

Actions are selected using a card at your turn, you can buy new card during the game. These cards are also associated with a god which will give point in the end of the game .

Multiple ways to score points : be present in differents countries, have a counter in different cities, unlock its boats and men, have money in the end part (even if it really is anecdotal), have counters producing a maximum of different resources and some others elements. All these points are multiplied by the number of cards of a certain god that was on your hand at the end of the game.

I did not really like the use of the cards to make points throughout the game we do not know who is in the lead because it is impossible to accurately calculate the score of each player. (unless you have an extraordinary memory and the time to do it...). So each one plays in his corner, and it is impossible to try to block a minimum the leader.

At 5 players, the game is relatively long and sometimes you will just wait your turn to take back yours cards in your hand.

RATING: 10
Very ingenious design. Deckbuilding elements but the cards you purchase go into your hand. So, there isn't a random shuffle and draw of the deck involved. Rather than a deckbuilder, it is more like a hand/action selection builder. Plus, not only are you developing your available actions throughout the game, but you are also creating your own scoring conditions that goes along with your strategy. Really provides multiple paths to victory. Rewards those that can plan two or three moves ahead.

For me the best game from Essen so far. Need to play this again quickly.

RATING: 8.4
I really adore this game for creating interesting gameplay and very tough decisions with such a simple ruleset. The theme is not that original nor is the art very good. The gameplay more than makes up for that. Interaction is mostly indirect and most of it is even positive. Getting resources when someone else uses the Prefect or using the Diplomat to copy a much needed action from an opponent. But there will certainly be times when your fellow gamers will make you wanna punch them. Building just where you was going to build and thereby making it too expensive for you or buying the cards you needed so badly just before you were going to.

All in all a great euro that's easy to learn and will give you lot's of replayability. This will be even more improved with the expansions. The new maps and especially the Salsa expansion will spice up the game even more.

RATING: 8
Lok: BHKJ0301TO
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This is a Mac Gerdts design that has a similar feel to some of his previous incarnations; albeit without a Rondel. Instead of a Rondel to choose actions, the new innovative concept encompasses cards that are used in a quasi deck/hand building manner to perform actions. 

You start with a set number of cards, but can buy more in an open market. Not only do the cards grant you more actions, but they also supply you with victory points at the end of the game dependent on fulfilling requirements stated on the cards.(e.g. houses built, provinces settled, type of goods you produce, number of workers on the map). The cards also have a victory point multiplier effect.

The entertaining aspect of the game is that while you are building your engine and spreading your dominion throughout the map, you want to acquire cards that bestow you points based on what you are doing. It is easy to lose sight of the bigger picture while building your engine.

Concordia is an elegant middle weight design with simple rules, thoughtful decisions, just the right amount of player interaction and a good dose of strategical depth. If you are a fan of Gerdts' designs, this won't disappoint.

RATING: 6
An interesting economic and set collection game.  The game is rondel-esque in that you have a hand of role cards and cannot re-use them until you play your Tribune card to get the used cards back.  That said, you can purchase more role cards to duplicate or modify your hand of roles.  It has  some area control aspects (benefits for owning businesses in certain regions) and a double-sided board to adjust the difficulty based on player count.  My impressions are currently favorable because it seems to have the right balance of luck, strategy, and player interaction.

RATING: 9.5
Love it! 

Trading in Mediterranean? Yes, I like the theme. Even if i didn't... this game is great!

For me, the best Mac Gerdts game ever (I still haven't played Imperial).

The way everything is intricated is brilliant. The deck building, the area control, resource management and the best part for me... the set collection. 

The game is pretty simple: you play a card, you execute the actions writen in the card, next player. Quick turns, where what other players do, do matter for your next actions, so you'll be entertained all the time. 
The interaction in Concordia is above average (comparing to this kind of games). Some cards replicate what other players do, other cards are worth of activating when other players do some actions first, etc etc

Strategy wise has depth, without being overcomplicated. By the end of the game it can be AP prone for some players, but not much of a big deal.

It's not that long and it plays well with the max amount of players.

Love this game!

RATING: 8
Another Mac Gerdts design. Enough for me as a recommendation. Looking forward to a first play...
++++++
Finally on the table, with three players (Italy map). All new to the game but as expected it is not hard to pick up and play moved along briskly. On the other hand, it is the kind of game where you need to play a couple of times to see how the dynamics evolve. Understanding the scoring is key and even after nodding our heads at the explanation, and understanding the multiplier nature of the Character Cards, the final scores after our first game brought a few surprises. It will be interesting to see how this experience will influence our future plays.

While the random seeding of the goods-producing cities, and the semi-random way the extra cards come out, is intended to ensure replayability, I think it will only be marginal. The initial fanning out of colonists will vary but once the players have established themselves on short, medium and long term goals the play should follow pretty familiar lines.

We are fans of all Mac Gerdts' designs and this game is solid and straight-forward enough to join those that will likely see play, but I'm wondering about its staying power. I expected that this would fit into the "medium-filler" category for us but the playing time may be somewhat longer than hoped for and may sink it. It is not enough game for the "main course" but may find its place when the main course ends sooner than usual.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I like the action selection mechanism (single cards from a replenishing hand) a great deal, as well as the personality card "tech." It is a super Euro-y game about economic development, with minimal direct conflict. But there is still interesting player interaction, with competition for personality cards and building priority, as well as the action mimicry of the Diplomat card.

The scoring system is unusual and not much susceptible to mid-play mental assessment. That makes the "intermediate scoring" mechanism very important for first-time players: Early in the game, scores are assessed, and then zeroed out, with small money bonuses going to the leaders at that point. In some respects, the scoring system reminds me of a more elaborate version of Kingdom Builder, with its reliance on calculation from the board state after the finish of play. 

I like how the scoring of the cards charts out a large but finite number of major strategies, allowing players to focus on one to three "gods."

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Fantastic. Along with Navegador, one of Mac Gerdts's best designs. I suppose deck-building/hand-management Euros are the new rage (e.g. Rococo, Lewis & Clark, City of Iron), but the fact that Concordia involves a map makes this game rise above the others, IMO. On top of the deck building, you have area control, transportation considerations, resource and money management, careful timing of actions to plan (including playing off of other players' actions), and you have to get a sense of the pace of the entire game to maximize end-game scoring. It's a dizzying and bewildering array of things to consider, but despite this, the game feels very light and fast because the turns churn out at a rapid pace.

Be forewarned, though, this is not just a "point salad" game, but a majority of the points are handed out at the end of the game. Until you're more experienced, you may not know who is winning until the winner is actually declared. I love this kind of game because it makes me feel like I'm doing well even when I am sucking royally, but it may turn some people off.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
An interesting game, and a bit unexpected for the subject matter!  The game is very Feld-ian, in that nearly everything you do in the game scores you points, and you of course have the goal of scoring the most points, so you're trying to optimize all of your point scoring.

However, the twist is that players have a hand of cards.  Each turn, you'll play a card, which allows you to do something (build civs, trade goods, produce goods, get more guys, etc).  However, the cards also have a point condition at the bottom that says you score for a certain thing.

Each player starts with identical cards, but during the game, you can get more of these cards.  So, not only are you adding actions to your hand that modify your ability to do things vs. the other players, but you are also modifying the way you score your points at the end of the game.  For example, some cards give you 2 points per guy you have.  If you manage to get 3 of these cards, you'll score 6 per guy, so you will want to focus on getting more guys.  However, another player may only have one of these cards, so they're better off working toward other goals.

In the end, you get a good combination of some mild deck building with a good base game that the cards drive.  Looking forward to playing more!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Shut Up & Sit Down really likes this game. I watched their review, and *literally fell asleep*. I then watched a playthrough on BGG Game Night and *fell asleep* again.

That being said, I bought it anyway. /r/boardgames and the two videos were so positive that I thought it'd still be worth a shot.

After our first play, I'm happy to say that the simple gameplay made it relatively easy to teach. There are *so* many options, though, that it's hard to tell what you should do.

On our first game, we didn't really concentrate on scoring. Both Patricia and I were spreading across Italy, staying out of each other's way and creating a trading empire.

The engine building of the game is really fun. Once you start getting a lot of cities and can produce a lot of goods, it feels like the game really starts to open up. However, because we weren't racing to buy cards, the two-player game took two hours. I'm sure we could have ended it sooner.

I hope to play with a larger group and see how it holds up.

Update: Played two more three-player games. The game feels really solid, and now that we're racing for cards, it feels like we're getting better at the game. Both three player games took 90 minutes, including setup.

Hoping to play a five-player game in the future.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
There isn't really a way to make this game [i]sound[/i] interesting. On paper, it sounds like any number of interchangeable Euros. But trust me, this is a terrific game. There's just no fat in Concordia - absolute minimal ruleset for maximum depth. There's a ton of tough decisions that only get more agonizing as the game goes on - but since you're limited to one action per turn (and further limited by cards in hand), there's not a lot of AP as players have an entire round to decide which to take (though of course, your opponents' decisions can affect your own!)

So yeah. Just play it. Don't read the description, don't look at the box cover (AVERT YOUR EYES), just play. Play and enjoy. Concordia is a treat.

Update: The more I play this, the more I realize 5p is just too many. The game really starts to drag at that count. If you're all really fast, then I could see it being fine. But personally I think I'll stick to 2-4 if I can (with 2 on the tiny expansion maps).

RATING: 6
My expectations were most likely too high – I had heard a lot of people claiming that this was as good as or even better than Navegador. However, it’s definitely not. The gameplay is not too interesting and it feels very repetitive. With 5 there is way too much downtime due to AP which means my enjoyment of the game naturally falls a lot. I thought I would like it much more with 3 or 4 (with 2 there wouldn’t be enough interaction and competition), but having played it with 3 too I cannot really say that it makes the game better. Actually there was less interaction due to fewer blocked cities and fewer expensive buildings. It doesn’t take as long as with 5 (about 1½ hours compared to 2½+), but the gameplay is still very repetitive as you kind of need to do some actions in a certain order. What I do like is how you acquire cards during the game and can play them and thereby choose your own actions and when to play them. This “rondel” you “build” out of your cards is quite interesting. You obviously have to keep a close eye on what your opponents do and are able to do as it affects your decisions.

It did kind of suck that the winner in my first 5p game took a lone road/are more or less by himself while others were closer to each other, meaning that the other players had to pay much more for the buildings; of course, that’s a tactical mistake by the other players, but the winning player actually also got no less than 8 cloth from one other player throughout the game – a lot of them at the beginning where he used the bonus action… I have to say that it made me slightly dissatisfied that one player could benefit so much from a suboptimal play by another player… And this mechanic in the game itself is definitely not something I enjoyed. Yes, of course you can build in areas where another player has a couple of houses so you’ll get a benefit when he uses the action where you get goods, but still… The player probably would have won anyway, but it’s not a given as he got a lot of essentially “free” cloth during the first couple of turns. Again, we played poorly, but the game let us do it. My next game didn’t have too much of this, though, so maybe it was just inexperience. 

The end game scoring is nicely done as you can get points in different ways depending on what your strategy is during the game. I like Navegador quite a lot, but Concordia was simply just not my cup of tea as it feels way too repetitive, constraining and, frankly, boring. And this comes from a hardcore eurogamer.

RATING: 8
Concordia is the third Gerdts game that I played after Imperial 2030 and Navegador. And, I really like all three. Like the other games, Concordia offers a streamlined set of rules, slick gameplay, and that fast "do an action--next player" flow of a turn.

So, Concordia is his non-rondel game, but the cardplay is still very much like a rondel, only customizable. You never shuffle and draw--all your cards represent your hand, and you choose the order you want to play them.

The customizing your hand is what makes this game different. There's not a market to manipulate per se, you simply cash in and buy resources at the stated price. It's all pretty straightforward, but there's plenty of decision making in the game. 

I want to play this again and I can see myself possibly picking this up at some point.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
So a deck-builder is sorta like a rondel? Well, that's what I tell myself, at least, about this gem of a game from the rondel king. I really like that Gerts has used a bit more uncertainty (using that word intentionally rather than luck) in this game compared to previous. The game has nice variation in setup to keep things fresh (more variation than most Gerts games). But it really is a deck builder. But unlike Trains, which is a deck builder with a map that barely matters, this one the map really matters--a lot. That's what you're actually thinking about. And there are (thankfully) [b][i]no card combos[/i][/b]. You simply play one card on your turn. Simple. Fast. Elegant (yes, I'm using that word). 

The scoring in this game is really genius. Every card you get (including your starting 7 base cards) has a god on the bottom. It doesn't affect game play at all. Rather, at the end of the game, you group all your cards by god, and that number is a [i]multiplier[/i] for the scoring condition associated with that god (7 gods, iirc). So when you're buying cards, you're looking mostly at what they do, but also how they score. You can get huge points by specializing. 

Another thing I like relative to most deckbuilders is that rather than having a static menu of cards, it's a sequence, with cards at the new end of the scale getting progressively more expensive than cards at the old end. Plus a cost on the card itself. It works really really well.

I may even bump this to a 9. We'll see. So far just really happy with first play. And I should mention really happy with how simple the core gameplay is: on your turn pick a card from your hand and do what it says. Rinse & repeat. That's it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

What a beautiful, elegant game. I'm a huge fan of the Classical theme and love reading the history of the cities that comes with the game. I love the hand management and the interaction as you try to out maneuver your opponent. I love the wooden bits and the beautiful, colorful boards. A top 10 game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The less rondel Mac puts in his games, the more I seem to like 'em. Concordia is a clear evolution of Navegador: the way the scoring mechanism works (by having players taking actions and collect the multipliers at the same time), the extra action going round counter-clockwise, the less pronounced but still discernable attempting to do what noone else is doing, ... . It's all there in one form or another. But it doesn't have an obvious rondel. 

Concordia was not on my radar because I suspected it came with deck building, a mechanism I intensely dislike, but as it turned out there is no deck building in sight. You have a growing stack of action cards from which you can freely choose. Okay, the stack grows and 'builds', but the free choice makes it fundamentally different. This is a Good Thing; and frankly, the presence of the Diplomat action which allows copying of the last previously played card of another player puts the icing on the cake as far as this mechanism goes. You definitely don't feel as boxed-in as in the older rondel games here, nor as in other action card with recall-titles. Okay, if I really want to be annoying I can state that the rondel is hidden in the action cards because they are single use only until you reclaim them, but since you add similar actions to your hand you can do a few repeats very easily. So in the end I'm inclined to say that this is a rondel-less game. At bloody long last.

The game itself is not really something to write extensive stories about. You walk around on a map with meeples and ships to erect little houses on cities: doing so gives you access to the resource that particular city produces which you can turn into cash or other resources. In order to erect new houses or gain new shipples [i]and[/i] (very important!) new action cards with the all-important multiplier bonuses you need different resources and [i]sic transit gloria mundi[/i]. There are no difficult patterns to master here, though there is some specialisation in that some will opt for quick movement and thus cheap access to yet unclaimed cities (they less costly to build in then), while others go for putting down all their houses in different provinces, yaddayaddayadda. Player interaction is less than in Navegador: instead of being really able to hurt someone after you in turn order, it's more of setting them up to do something different which is still worthwhile. Concordia has a very friendly feel to it, even at the maximum of 5 players.

What I really dislike about this game though is the fact that keeping track of how well the other players are doing is a muddled mess. In Navegador you can still see what people are after; not so in Concordia because the card decks are kept in a player's hand, thus obscuring the important multiplier information. I would be in favour of having an information table somewhere where everything except the different Minerva cards is tracked. This is not, by the way, a running score, because tracking that is a convoluted mess. But something akin to Navegador is certainly welcome. This allows much more precise blocking and playing, which only adds to the game as a whole in my opinion. It's easy to make such a little board, which has me wondering why it wasn't included with the game to begin with. Perhaps it makes the game too chewy? In any case, this severely detracts from the game's overall pleasant feel, and costs it 1 point, undeservedly. 

Concordia is not a game I need to own, but as I said it has the best papers of arriving in my collection under the right circumstances. It only took Mac 6 or 7 games to finally make a title which begins to agree with my tastes :meeple:. Come on, Mac: next time make a game with unrestricted action choice. It'll be fun!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Don't play with five!

RATING: 6
I ended the rules explanation for Concordia *really* excited to play. And the game was fun... but when it finished I was overall less excited about it, primarily for two reasons:

1. The board felt too big. We had 4 players on the 2-4 player side, and it felt like there was *always* room to spread out into uncontested territory. There's certainly reason to be 2nd or even 3rd into a city - leeching goods off of a region that someone else is likely to produce in seems lucrative - but I was expecting/hoping the board would grow uncomfortably crowded by endgame, forcing hard decisions about where to produce, and that simply didn't happen. Three of us still had private or mostly-private fiefdoms, either maintained for the game or built after primary fiefdoms were encroached upon. 

(This may have been in part because one of our four players built very few cities, but we still had as many on the board as you'd expect in a 3-player game.)

2. One player came in a strong 2nd place despite building something like 3 cities, mostly on the strength of having all of his colonists and all three of the Mars cards. On the one hand, it's nice that players can pursue different strategies. On the other hand, I find the strategy of "just build colonists" to be uninteresting - and on first glance, it seems like the Mars cards are something you *cannot* afford to let one player buy all of. I expect this will balance itself out with experienced players, since the Mars cards are apt to be worth a minimum of 6-10 points each (unless there are low-colonist strategies?), but the dynamic irritates me. I'm hoping scores will in general be higher than they were in our game, to help make the Mars cards more of a side dish.

Enjoyed just about everything else about the game: resource/storage management, hand/action management, having money flow through my fingers like water (never quite enough)...

RATING: 8
Still enjoy this with 2p.  In Concordia, you really are faced with difficult decisions every step of the way, even though the turn structure and overall rules are very simple.  The biggest decisions, we've found, come from producing with the Prefect - which usually involves giving the other player free goods.  But even the path you take through the map, the cards you draft, and the scoring strategies you aim for, intertwine in many different ways and lead to many difficult decisions.

Through these decisions, Concordia offers what we generally look for in a strategy game: you're always faced with the option of going in a different direction, and have quality choices presented to you that can lead to other lines of strategy.  What is impressive is that it does this without a whole lot of variability (compared to, e.g., Brew Crafter's cards, or RFTH) - it all comes from the city tiles, the province bonuses, and, of course, the market row.

RATING: N/A
$59 Amazon
Concordia is a peaceful strategy game of economic development in Roman times for 2-5 players aged 13 and up. Instead of looking to the luck of dice or cards, players must rely on their strategic abilities. Be sure to watch your rivals to determine which goals they are pursuing and where you can outpace them! In the game, colonists are sent out from Rome to settle down in cities that produce bricks, food, tools, wine, and cloth. Each player starts with an identical set of playing cards and acquires more cards during the game.

RATING: 9
An economic theme based on the deck building mechanic, with lots of strategic ways to score points. 
The game prodution is neat and the game plays faster than one might think. There is enough interaction between players, as each one is rushing either to claim spots (cities) on the board or to buy improvment cards.
The push your luck mechanics regarding the income in the provinces (flip tiles) is also nice. 
Besides, the timing is a crucial aspect of this game, because attention must be paid regarding the best use of the "copy action" cards and the resources available for each player at any given time, so that they can not take advantages of your own actions... 
Overall, the game is a very pleasent surprise!
Mac Gerdts reach my top with a game where the rondel is not present, who would figure that out, hein?!...

RATING: 9
Interesting game with an excellent scoring system that completely makes the game as good as it is.  Cards that you use to carry out your actions are also used at the end of the game to determine your score, this allows you to customize the strategy that you are attempting to carry out while playing the game, and that information is completely visible to the other players at the table. Taking cards is where the real interaction in this game lies, the board itself is a little bit more passive than I'd generally like it to be. The game also has a race element to it, and the first player to end the game gets a sizeable bonus to their score.

Minor criticisms, the penalty for building in another's regions, especially for low cost goods, is too minimal and makes the game less cut-throat than it could be.  I also wish there were more varieties of card that could be purchased with the senators, and potentially more than one Minerva card for each type of good, allowing for more extreme specialization.  I'm very interested in the expansion, but I'd also love to see a game use a similar scoring mechanism that is longer and more complex.

RATING: 7

RATING: 9
Players are .... I'm not sure what players are.  I guess some sort of well to do Roman, doing what Romans did - building a network of contacts across the empire, in a bid to become more powerful.

Players play cards from their hand to take actions - accumulate resources, cash, plant houses, move their pieces by land and sea, and acquire more cards.  They gradually build up a deck of cards by the end of the game that have in built scoring multipliers (bit like the Stone Age cards) for end of game scoring.

Perfectly solid, workmanlike game - but awfully dry, with a weird tempo.  The first two thirds of the game are glacial as you try and build an economic engine with your cards and game board network.  Then suddenly the game leaps away and seems to end very quickly.  Happy to play it again, needs to be explored further.

Repeated plays has convinced me this is an excellent game.  Very clean and easy to teach, but not easy to play well.  It does teach you to be efficient, and not waste a turn.

RATING: 9
This is a great game, that plays well at all the counts (2-5) - I don't feel like much, if anything, is lost in the two player game, and with the salsa expansion adds another map to play on, so there are lots of map choices now.
I think that overall the game is quite austere, but I feel that the card 'fake rondel' mechanic makes the game as turns can be 'instantaneous', and without players who get AP the game can zip along, after a the first few quite scripted turns. Also the game is very generous as in you can one way or another get the resources you want, you are also not blocked from cities once someone else builds there so you are never denied anything, it just becomes more expensive for you, bit like life really.
Set up time is medium, and you can easily delegate jobs to speed it up.
It's a weird one to teach however as it's deceptively (or maybe not deceptively) easy to teach, basically you play a card and do what it says - with a few caveats and stuff you might forget. However I would recommend doing intermediate scoring if playing with new people as it is easy to have no idea how you are doing, and to lose based on buying no Minerva cards or Mars cards even though that aside from that you have played well, or excellently.
Conclusion: I think this is a solid and to use an oft overused word - elegant game, though I'd rather play a handful of Euros over it, and though I think the board is gorgeous the box art - smiling woman with grapes in ancient world -  doesn't bring anyone to the yard.

Update: almost a ten, this game is a winner in all my play groups.

RATING: 7
Solid medium-weight Eurogame that would be near the top of my purchase list if I was still buying Euros hand over fist.   Blends a lot of familiar mechanisms together - role selection, resource engine, network building, and even a little bit of deck building.   Couple of clever things here.   Each card has a scoring condition attached to the bottom, so each and every card in your deck will get you points in some way.  In order to build settlements that produce X, you must first have X to build the settlement.  So, it's an interesting chicken and egg puzzle that requires use of a Mercator or a Prefect to solve.   Engaging game, even with a full 5 players.   Maybe not particularly original, but well blended.  Odd box size and ugly cover art are minor problems, but those aren't too important.

RATING: 6
A decent resource management/planning game.  I think you really have to get going out of the gate, putting cities on the board so that you can benefit more from production.  Then you've got to expand from there, while simultaneously buying cards, since the game can end pretty quickly.  This sort of balance between cities and the scoring cards which count them is difficult to achieve.
The cards are probably the best part of the game.  It's not a deckbuilder, but it has a little of that feel, since your choices narrow down until you play the card that resets everything back to your hand.  Each new card has an ability, but it also has a scoring criterion.  Hopefully, you're picking up cards whose scoring formulae you're going to try and max out.  
There are the usual problems.  Some of the criteria you can pretty much forget about (money is just an afterthought), others are very tough to do (getting a city in every province), and some are really easy (the Mars cards that just require you to unlock 4 pieces).  I also think the specialist cards are pretty worthless.
It should be a good game despite this.  But for me, it never escapes the feeling of just another boring resource-management exercise.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
If you form a strategy and take a few turns to execute it, it may be foiled or helped by another player in a way that feels accidental to both of you. It's very Euro in that way, but the possibility of being influenced does provide some tension.

Interaction is fairly low, with only a few opportunities for clever plays that would help you while intentionally disadvantaging others. Knowing the cards well can increase these opportunities.

Enjoyment mostly comes from puzzling out the optimal way divide your strategy into efficient turns. There's endgame tension when your last plan is threatened by the possibility of someone triggering the endgame, or if you are in a position to trigger it, whether you should do it or try to earn more points first.

I didn't like this the first few times, because I felt pressure to estimate everyone's hidden-but-trackable scoring formulas and act accordingly. After playing the game half a dozen times, I have more of an internalized feel for who's going after what and what will efficiently increase my score. It takes several games to get there, which I consider a knock.

RATING: N/A
[b][FirstImpression20thNov2013][/b]Where do I even start. As a Mac Gerdts fan, this feels similar (in a good way; his signature designs are in there), yet different. The 'rondel' is now the cards that you recycle in your hand.

There are five different resources in the game, but I don't know which one to focus on while in the game. Everything seems important but not all are easy to achieve. Cloth/silk is important to buy cards, brick is important for foundation to build building, tools and wheat/food are important to bring in new colonies. Every resources is important but I don't think focusing on all is a good idea. 

Cards that you purchase will shape how you will score at the end of the game, so basically you also want to focus in what your cards will score for you. 

For those who are familiar with Mac Gerdts designs, you will see some similarities to his other designs (note this doesn't mean a bad thing at all). The 'rondel' mechanic is still there, but there are now switched to cards instead of the actual rondel. The 'no points are scored' while the game is in progress is there, so basically only when the game ends we will score for VPs. The ship and person wooden tokens are in similar shape as his previous games.  

I like that he didn't use cubes to represent the materials, so you can recognize the resources right from their shape. 

So far I have to say I like it although I still need more play just to see how much I like it.

[b][SecondPlayOn21stNov2013][/b]Played a 2 player game. I can confirm that it works although the dynamic has now changed. With 2 players, you can focus more in certain areas, but not in multiplayer games.

In our 2 player game, my opponent ended the game by buying the last 2 cards, and with my last and final turn, I built up all my 15 buildings (which is the other end game condition). 

The game ends in about an hour.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Concordia is a Mac Gerdts design, he of Navigador and the rondel. Cornucopia gets rid of the rondel and instead is a fascinating map-based, card-based objective-driven game which works elegantly and with a lot of tension. The rules are few, just 4 pages, and upon reading I was underwhelmed. But when you play the game you realise just how clever and exciting it becomes. 

Points are scored according to a number of common game-end objectives (number of cities you are in, amount of money etc). You all have an identical deck of cards and the cards give particular actions on the map (expand, gain resources, trade etc) but once played you can't do that action again until you choose to effectively forfeit an action and pick up the cards. 

So far so dull...but here's the interesting bit: you can get more cards (thus building a deck) and these same cards also act as points multipliers to the different respective objectives at the end of the game. The conflict is thus: you want to build a diverse deck to give you a good choice of actions, but in order to maximise your scoring multipliers at the end you have to concentrate on one or two of the objectives and therefore you need to concentrate on collecting one or two types of card multipliers. But this gives you a deck with loads of the same action, which is not useful at all. How you balance your actions deck whilst trying to achieve the multipliers for one or two objectives is the key to the game. And the tension comes from the fight for space on the board, for the cards that come up and for money & resources. Tense tense tense. And the beauty is that, unless you are keeping track of every card each person buys you really have no idea who is doing well until the final scoring so there is no sense of playing a losing game. 

Fabulous game, and one of the very best from 2013.

RATING: 3
I really like the graphic design on this game, even the box art.  It's nice to see a game that doesn't have an amped up masculine design on the cover.  The map is beautiful and the pieces are excellent.  I like the cards, but the text is a little more than I care for.  I imagine that once I get to know the cards better there will be less reading.   

Come first play, I was really excited to play this.  From the rules, it seemed like a sleek, fast playing euro.  I also have a fond love of games that use cards to drive play, so this game was at the top of my anticipated game list for 2013.  Then I played it.  It's not a fast playing game.  You need to really keep an eye on the scoring possibilities and keep them in the back of your head.  You also need to dig into your hand of cards, figure out what you want to do, and map it out among the available actions.  

This game is a thinker, and you need to wade through all the text on the cards and line it all up in the right order, like a neatly structured essay, in order to get the things you want done.  I don't really know if I had fun playing this game.  There's a lot of thinking, but all the actions are dirt simple.  There's not a lot of meat to chew on here.  Just a lot of text to arrange.  It feels like a skeleton of a game.

On top of that, this game made me look at all my other games and question why I have all these stupid things, and made me not want to play them.  I think it has to do with the bare bones structure of the presentation.  The actions on the cards reflect the general decision making processes that exist in most games.  It was like peeling back the skin and muscle and looking at the bones, and then being unable to see anything but bones when looking at other games.  Maybe I'm just burned out on games.  I dunno.  This game messed me up though and it should probably get a special award for that.

RATING: 7
I think it is a solid game, with really easy rules for teaching.  It seems like it would be good regardless of whether you play from 3 - 5.  Two may even be decent but I would just rather play one of my other two player games.  I find there to be a lot of indirect interaction in this one: taking cards from the display that others want, jockying to end the game for bonus points, trying to build in places before your opponents so they have to pay more to build in the same city, trying to build in regions where your opponents are so when they produce you will get something out of it, and of course paying attention to what others have played so you can diplomat appropriate cards.  I like that the only scoring is done at the end of the game, so it's kind of hard to tell who's winning.  One would think that a game with cards would have great replay value, but there is very little randomness to the cards.  You use the same cards based on the number of players, it's just the order in which they come out in the display that differs (which technically could change things up).  While it's a solid game, I worry that it will feel very samey after 5 - 7 games, so it may not be long for my collection.

RATING: 8
Concordia is everything its cracked up to be. While the theme is exhaustively overdone, Concordia twists the Euro game mold is two distinct ways, which lend directly to its popularity. Firstly, player interaction while indirect is not obstructive, and is instead (mostly) beneficial to others. Secondly, and more importantly, players tailor their own end game scoring values. This promotes drastically varied play, and equips players with an incredible amount of control. Once the learning curve is cleared the game moves briskly, and rarely overstays its welcome.

RATING: 4
Pros
--Variable setup allows games to feel a lot less samey
--Grows with you with familiarity (I don't think I'll ever get to that point as I generally don't like this game)
--Definitely don't feel funneled to one single path to victory

Cons
--Unintuitive scoring, only offering feedback at the end
--Must jump through hoops (unintuitive economy/trading process) to complete desired actions
--Nearly every action you do gives you points, but at different rates for different reasons.  This is the worst point salad possible.  The least objectionable point salads give immediate feedback.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The interesting thing about this game is that it really seems like a mid 90's Euro from the pre-engine building era, yet it's from 2014 and I enjoy having it in my collection for that fact. It's not my favorite Euro, but it's pretty unique. The rules are simple, but the practical strategies are pretty deep. 

One of the appeals of this game for many people is that while it's a competitive game where you're actions can effect other people, there's really not any direct or even indirect conflict (ie, worker blocking). This is not really a concern of mine, and honestly this game does seem a bit tame & friendly. It can seem anti-climactic, rather than ramping up and getting exciting towards the late game.

I have also had trouble with scoring in this game. It should be straight forward, but people always get hung up on it. It makes no sense to have a scoring track for a game that only scores at the end, especially in a game with multiple score categorized with different multiplication values applied to each one. I fully believe that this game should have come with scoring sheets for itemized score recording, rather than a scoring track.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Concordia is a reasonably good euro with a unique handbuilding mechanism that offers a different experience from the usual worker placement. Tension is created via the need to take cards on offer vs get resources vs spend resources to take board spaces before they become much more expensive. Cards you acquire go right into your hand for use immediately, and building your deck of actions is reasonably fun. 

On the downside, the heuristics in this game are tough. The ostensible fun of the game is taking actions that are either engine-building or VP-scoring -- but you don't know how well you're doing because scoring happens at game end. I have a hard time knowing what's a better relative move during the game; and after the game, it's not clear what I should have done. Some of my friends are great at it, however. 

And I'm OK with that as a game to play on occasion, but the shine of the card collecting wore off quick, like 4 plays, and I sold my copy. I'll play on occasion, but the overall experience felt thin awfully soon.

Update: After trying the Salsa expansion, I think they addressed the thinness of the experience by adding in special actions and powers.

RATING: 9
One of the easiest games to slip into in recent memory, I'm truly impressed at the smooth gameplay. Despite my unwarranted concerns about the themed setting, it's a delight. 

Basically you are performing actions, hand management, a little deck building, worker placement, resource investment/production/trading...and yes a personalized rondel (your hand of cards, think Runewars). Add to that your hand is actually a recipe for "point salad", and you'll still be surprised at how charming it is.

And yes, there is luck due to randomized locations, bonuses and a shuffled deck...if only he could've figured out how to add dice and combat :)

RATING: 8
Concordia manages to combine many mechanics into one cohesive and compelling game.  While you're deck/hand building, the game gives you incentive to hold off on shuffling as long as possible.  You must wander the countryside expanding your network, but having the largest network does not guarantee you the win.  You can collect all that money from the territories, but in doing so you provide your opponents resource opportunities.

Incredibly balanced game that only gets better with every game you play.

RATING: 6.5
DOWNGRADE:

There is a scoring problem with this game. It looks like SATURNUS and JUPITER cards are the way to go, with other cards being underpowered, namely VESTA's.

The thing is that SATURNUS and JUPITER cards synergize with other things in the game, whereas e.g. MARS cards don't. Just think how expensive it is to put all your workers on the board. And for what... For only trying to multiply their numbers by the MARS cards you get? Totally not worth it. You DON'T need 6 workers on the board.

To me MERCURIUS and MARS cards should read "3 points for every...".

RATING: 7
Concordia reuses the card play from Flandern 1302 and extends it a little so you can add more cards to your hand. Ironically, while this is probably Concordia's most distinguishing feature the scoring that is firmly tied to the acquisition of those cards is also the one feature that feels tacked on and entirely disjointed from the boardplay. In other words, what you do during the game only has a fairly weak connection with what you actually score at the end. The most impressive merchant empire is worth zilch if you didn't buy the right cards in sufficient quantities.

Other than that the game feels very solid yet a little old-fashioned which isn't necessarily a bad thing. Sometimes it is good enough simply not to be objectionable.

RATING: 7
CONCORDIA ist ein Spiel, indem sich Kreise so ineinander verschrauben, dass man zunächst nicht weiss, um welchen Kreisel man sich zuerst kümmern soll. Beispiel: man braucht genau diejenige Ressource, mit der man ein Euro-Holzhäuschen in eine Stadt pflanzen kann, die diese Ressource produziert. Ich bekomme die Ressource für den Bau des Euro-Holzhäuschens, wenn ich bereits ein Holzhäuschen in der Stadt gebaut habe, die diese Ressource produziert. Die sprichwörtliche Katze, die sich in den eigenen Schwanz beisst, beschreibt das ganze Spielgefühl von CONCORDIA, indem alles mit allem kreisend verschraubt ist, so dass sich das Rondell von Mac Gerdts, das er in CONCORDIA diesmal durch Karten ersetzt hat, sich im eigenen Kopf dreht und einen anfänglichen Schwurbel verursacht. Drehungen, das man einem komisch wird, ist die Eigenschaft von Karussell, welches ganz schön Spaß machen kann, wenn man sich an die Drehungen gewöhnt hat. Für alle anderen bleibt nur die Ressource Papierkotztüte. Das Schnarchthema Rom mit dem kleinen Latinum ist natürlich boring, wird aber durch die alternativen Spielpläne aufgeweicht.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9

RATING: 9
Excellent euro game.  It uses a very interesting deck mechanism.  Each turn you play a single card of your choice, but also control when you want to recall your used cards back into your deck, but it will cost you a turn.  Timing your moves is tricky and since scoring is based on a combination of board state and the cards in your hand at the end of the game, it creates a fun challenge to consider your end-state strategies while pursuing mid-game objectives.

RATING: 6
I've wanted to try Concordia for awhile. I have yet to meet a Mac Gerdts game that truly blows me away, so I was hoping that this one would be one. In the end, I think I still like Navegador a shade more than Concordia. Some interesting mechanics that worked together quite well: playing a hand of cards as actions, collecting more cards to increase available actions, and using those cards as scoring multipliers at the end of the game. But with 2 hours of play the game ends feeling quite dry and repetitive. Never really felt any tension either. The game was more often than not an exercise in resource and card management. I never really felt like that special "something" was created when naturally dry Euro mechanics come together. There are a great number of Euros that I enjoy and are able to bring some magic. Perhaps I just felt Concordia had no magic. But it certainly was a solid game and it all works. Nothing clunky or weird or overly gamey. Just a little too dry to inspire my want to play it again anytime soon.

This game is part worker placement and feels a bit like a deck builder (buying cards which go into your hand, give you powers and score VP at game's end).  This game works really well with four people.  It's competitive - I came in second by ONE point - but there are always good moves to take on your turn.  Turns are quick unless you play with AP prone players.  Really look forward to playing this one again and trying out the expansions.

RATING: 8.5
Concordia has a nice peaceful element to it, with indirect confrontation (frustrating other players by beating them to cities and cards). The board, pieces, and cards are nicely crafted & designed and bring one into the theme pretty convincingly. Some say it it dry and it is, but the cardplay keeps you engaged well enough. The only problem, as I see it, is the unused scoring track around the board (unused until the end when scores are added). There's simply no way to know where players are with regard to points (although you can estimate). I suppose once you get used to the game & are familiar with the god-cards scoring mechanism, you can watch for what cards players have & keep track, but until then- a bit frustrating.
All that being said, it's nowhere near as good a game as Imperial or Imperial 2030.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Such a brilliant game! The rules are incredibly simple but the decisions every turn are interesting and strategical. It's a mix of small, interesting mechanics that fit together using some of my favourite mechanics, hand management and deck building. There are plenty of paths to success which leads to feeling like every move is good and as the game continues your choices will decrease, which leads to less AP. The diplomat is my favourite card, because it allows you a "free" action if your opponent plays something you already wanted to do. However that may not always be the best move, as the diplomat can allow you to play a rare card, such as senator, twice if you hold on to it. 

I'm not a fan of the theme, but the components are nice and the box size is lovely.

RATING: 7
Liked it much better on my second play with three than my first play with two. 

Three players might be the sweet spot but I'd be up for trying a four-player game. 

PROS/NOTES
-A dynamic push and pull between when to work on your engine and when to take points.
-A surprising amount of planning in the resource management. 
-A big push to get many different things done in a short amount of time. I was often having to take several turns to set up for one action. 
- All this led to some turns being quite quick where others were more AP prone. Part of this is due to the fact that it's hard to plan for your next turn as the board can change drastically and you might need to make a plan B on the fly. The main reason why this might work better at three.

CONS
-It's quite hard to have a strategy for choosing cards. It just seems like you end up taking whatever cards you can afford and hoping for the best. And this leads me to the fact that...
-My biggest complaint is I don't like the endgame scoring but I don't see a reasonable way around it considering the cards are multipliers.

RATING: 9.3
Just your general, every day, set collection, area control, hand and resource management game. Despite the hodge podge of mechanics, I LOVED it. It's going to go into my top 10 after one play. Yes, the theme is non-existent, but the board is vibrant and colorful and the game provides such wonderful tension. You never feel like you are making the optimal move, even when you are. You never feel like you are doing all you want to do, even if you are accomplishing more than your opponents. I don't love that all the scoring occurs in the end game giving you no idea where you stand along the way, but that is a minor quibble. This game is awesome.
