=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[b]Concordia is one of my favourite game from my collection, the 1st game I rated 10. This game reeks elegance.[/b]

I first read about Concordia on a Geeklist and that time I was quite knew to the BG land. Was in an impulsive buying spree. Then I saw this [url=https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=X9u6IuoIPUA]review by Shut Up & Sit Down[/url] and was sold then and there. I wanted this game and it was out of print. Searched for it a lot and it was really expensive to get. The shops in Tokyo were teasing me with the Japanese edition of Concordia and then finally got my hands on it later when it appeared in UK stores.

[b]Reasons I own it:[/b]
Concordia is a one of the first few games where I actually started researching gameplay before buying, unlike few games that I got just based on review alone.


- Primary reason would be Quinns review on Concordia got me intrigued
- The rulebook, damn - that’s freaking awesome
- Highly respected game on BGG (doesn’t mean I should like it, but again interest piqued)
- Simplicity and the depth of the game
- I love the puzzle it offers with the cards
- My first hand-management game
- The scalability of the game with player count
- An excellent 2 player game

[b]Components and presentation:[/b]

This is one aspect of the game I was tad bit disappointed with. No, not the artwork but the components for a $60 game is quite sub-par in my honest opinion. Yes, I know I am paying for the [b]design of the game[/b] but, [i]Hey![/i] As a consumer it’s my right to complain.
However, To be fair it is not as nearly lazily produced like Terraforming Mars ([i]yes, I just said that - TM has one of the worse production to price ratio[/i]) and I really like Concordia’s thick board. 
Concordia, a card game, then why not have a bit higher quality cards. Okay maybe it gets too expensive to produce but there are other games giving linen-finish card for lesser price range.

The wooden tokens/meeples are like any euro, with cheap paint applied on it where you can start seeing through the paint. Probably the only two exceptions for quality meeples even from the paint’s standpoint ([i]at least in my collection[/i]) are [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/boardgamepublisher/23202/stonemaier-games]Stonemaier Game’s[/url] meeples and [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/boardgamepublisher/10663/quined-games]Quined Game’s meeples.[/url]. Other than that, the cardboard pieces are quite thick and sturdy.

Now, the artwork; Well this is so subjective, even more so than a review of a game. Concordia’s artwork isn’t for everyone. I like the board’s artwork, the graphic style of the cards and tiles too. Okay, I will be honest it grew on me. First time when I saw it, it had the look of a burned dry bread, I thought it’s a bland, uninteresting looking board. But like I mentioned, the more I played, the more I started liking the artwork; Why though? Because it’s consistent, that’s why. Unlike Terraforming Mars, where half is drawn and the other half is google or stock images with ugly graphics overlay Concordia is extremely consistent in its presentation. 
The board has this quirky details if you care to study is closely, the sea region is detailed with [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/image/3958775/govindk?size=original]weird creatures and ships[/url] and there is even a [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/image/3958776/govindk]creepy looking mermaid[/url] staring back at you. The [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/image/3958777/govindk]Pantheon icons[/url] which has represents the city, with the letters for good tiles on top, also has a tiny letter and the name “CONCORDIA” inscribed on it’s pediment.
The whole board is like a painting on a marble tile if you look at the texture of the board carefully. So all the mural style art was intentional, it wasn’t a lazy design decision by the artist as I initially inferred.

***NOTE: STILL EDITING.***

RATING: 9
Of the three 2014 KennerSpiel nominees for 2014, which include Istanbul and Rococco, this is the one that I think I'll get to the table the most often. I've enjoyed all of them equally and feel that in terms of their complexity, levels of interaction and conflict they are close. However I believe Concordia is the one which lighter gamers will enjoys the most. It's theme and central map board and mechanics resonate easily with more familiar traditional games such risk or Settlers of Catan. The turns are quick; the game is generous with resources and players can never completely block one another. Truly frustrating turns are rare and players always feel that they are making some progress even if they are not necessarily winning. In short this game does not feel like 'mean' game and would be a strong non-conflicting couples game. 
The endgame scoring takes a bit to grasp which is both good and bad. It keeps the game tense and players involved as you are never completely sure who's in the lead. However novice players should have the importance of the gaining more personality cards highlighted. Not Only do these cards progress their in-game strategy but  critically they increase their endgame score. My opponent for my first game dominated more areas of the board and had better resources generated but failed to claim victory as I had simply more personality cards to score with at the end of the game.

UPDATE: Istanbul with all the expansions (Grand Bazaar variant) might be the slightly better game but has a bit higher bar to entry.

RATING: 9
This is my favourite kind of Euro: a relatively small ruleset belying the depth of the game. This is a heavy game, but it's heavy because you've got to think a lot while you play, not while you try to figure the rules out. Theme-wise it's not the greatest achievement (but it's not as dry as, says, Hansa: the theme does not seems to be a complete afterthought and the gameplay does not feel completely disconnected from it). However, the gameplay is very engrossing and being able to explain it easily to newcomers is always a welcome quality. Do not expect to be able to win against experienced players on your first play though.
I can't wait to try the expansion.

RATING: 5
While I haven't played too many of Gerdts' games, I was still surprised to see that there was no rondel in Concordia! In my opinion, this is done in good form as well - the game uses a hand of cards which limits what actions you use. Interestingly, you only begin with 1-2 cards for each type of action (there's only 6), and you must spend a turn to recoup all of them. As an interesting twist, the recouping incentivizes you to have played most of your hand in order to collect money in the process (i.e., for each card recouped above 3, claim a coin). This alone encourages more balanced and interactive game rather than hyper-specialized play (i.e., the kind where everyone picks a path to victory and hope no one else picked the same strategic path). As the game progresses, players also differentiate slightly their abilities (e.g., having an additional merchant action) which leads to some differentiation, but it's more minor than major as every player has a card in their hand that allows them to copy one ability (which becomes increasingly useful to copy a slightly superior version of an action; e.g., merchant gives you 5 additional coins to work with rather than 3). Furthermore, these cards also slightly modify your endgame scoring conditions (e.g., you get two points per dude on the map, however, you multiply this by the number of cards of that type you have in your hand). The rest of the game is typical Gerdts stuff, travel+occupy, produce, buy/sell on a map of the Mediterranean. Like with other Gerdts games, the action moves at a fair pace, everything is broken down into parts such that you have some idea of what is coming from other players (and potentially have a chance to respond in kind, unless you too have to mount a response that simply won't come soon enough). While it is possible to keep track of the score throughout the game, I'm quite alright with the big reveal at the end rather than the incremental scoring throughout (e.g., like Tigris & Euphrates) as scoring does take a bit of time and to have to keep track of micro-changes throughout might slow the pace down quite a bit. Overall, a well produced game with gamey moments throughout.

RATING: 8.2
起始設置: 洗混30張城市指示物，英文字母朝上再翻開貨物種類。
配置獎勵標記，將每個省分的最有價值貨物放置標記欄位。
設置卡片依照I~V排列(移除數字比人數大之卡片)，玩家拿取2食物1布料1酒1磚塊1工具以及兩工人兩船，從首家開始5/6/7/8元，尾家有大長官卡。
行動:
1.建築師: 移動殖民者，移動結束不可停留在被占據路上，移動後可以建造房屋，建造依造幫助卡上所需花費，若有其他城市數量，乘上房屋數量倍數。
2.長官: 選擇該省分獎勵標記生產貨物(必須要顯示貨物那面)，並且獲得獎勵標記之貨物，每個房屋都會生產一個。或者選擇金錢獎勵，每個獎勵錢幣計算完後拿取，之後所有標記翻回貨物那面。
3.殖民者:花1食物及1工具，可以將新殖民者放到羅馬或者自己房屋的城市裡。或者得5元，版圖上每有一個殖民者，多得1元。
4.莫卡托:銀行領3元，交易貨物，買或者賣選兩種，可以都買或都賣
5.外交官: 複製一張其他玩家棄牌堆上的卡，可以複製自己。
6.眾議員: 購買名人卡(最多兩張)，需花費卡片上方紅色區域貨物以及遊戲圖版上，該卡片下方印的貨物，問號可以任選。
7.執政官:購買名人卡(一張)，不須支付板塊下方物品。
8.專家:生產玩家該貨物(有房屋)
計分:(每種分數X張數)
1.每10元一分，無條件捨去
2.朱比特:每棟不在磚塊城市的房屋1分
3.薩圖爾奴斯: 每棟房屋1分
4.默丘利:每種生產的貨物2分
5.馬爾斯:每個殖民者2分
6.米納瓦依貨物張數取得分數

RATING: 9.5
The first few turns are pretty scripted in this game (player 1 will build and everyone else will copy or play their build card). However, everyone will most likely have built in separate places due to how the cost of building in a shared city multiplies.

I love the blend of using cards as actions and as end-game point multipliers. Kind of a given, but you will lose if you do not buy more cards over the course of the game. There is a bit of an internal tug of war--do you go for a particular card first or do you build in that cloth city before someone else does and causes the price to double?

Oh and there are several maps and they are all good! The main distinction is some maps are "best" for lower player counts or visa versa. The easiest way to tell is look at how many provinces are on that map (via the bonus good section).

RATING: 8
Navegador's cousin. Whether that's good or bad is up to you, of course. The way the map works is much more multifaceted and gives players lots of ways to get around; I also liked the harvest mechanism. This is one of three (!) games this year to use the no-shuffle deckbuilding mechanic, something I hadn't seen before.  I don't know what my opinion of this will be in the long run, but I'm definitely interested in playing it more. (1 play)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
If you haven't tried Concordia yet, please put it at the top of your games to play list.  I am trying to help you.  The game is awesome.  This is a wonderful economic Euro game with brilliant game play mechanisms combined in a superlative composition.  There is no luck or randomness in Concordia.  There is no direct confrontation or "take that" in the game as well.  Players are tasked with managing resources and establishing colonies across the map in order to maximize their final score.  Players must be mindful when adding to their deck of action cards because the cards also each have a particular scoring property that rewards one of six different categories: number of different resources produced or total non-brick colonies or number of different provinces occupied, and so on.  I love the map and the components and the game length and the flow of the game.  This is a strong contender for the perfect game and it's every bit as good or better than any game I have ever played.  The level of player interaction is quite good too.  There is no attacking or destroying other players' stuff, but there is a very serious competition mainly for new cards, but also for unoccupied colonies.  I can't say enough good things about Concordia and I can't think of anything I don't like about the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Super area domination game where you can share other people's developments and ride off their efforts.  Sliding card purchases (like Lewis & Clark) is really nicely balanced providing very important individualized scoring paths & strategies (what makes this game so good).  Little bit of a long game and initially a steep learning curve.  Can be very hard to figure out who is winning as the points are calculated at the end of the game and based on collected cards from during the game (the senator action is vital to getting these cards and cannot be ignored).

RATING: 7
Concordia reflects Roman Empires. Especially, the game is focused on Roman commercial network. Players try to optimize their actions with their character cards. It is very important how properly use the action cards. Playing character action card is more than a halfway importance of the game. This is the most noticeable advantage of Concordia. With the simple rules, Concordia can be appealed to light or new boardgamers too. In fact, I could definitely say that this game is not an easy game. The rule is easy, but the tactical choices are hard and tough. Of course, I have few doubts about the game. I am not sure whether the theme of the game is smoothly working or not. In addition, I don't think the game provides enough fun compare with Imperial or Antike. Perhaps, with simple rules, I wonder the game is lack of absorptive power to gamers. Especially compared with Mac Gerdts' former Rondel games, Concordia is not enought to captivate gamers attention. I guess Rondel is still a nice mechanic to be appealed to many boardgame lovers. In fact, Mac Gerdts' strenghs were fully stood out from Rondel games. I am not setting any limits to Gerdts magnetism. I totally agree Concordia is a nice game. Though, at the same time, I need to say Concordia is still no match for Mac Gerdts' former masterpieces. As time goes by, Concordia is going to be evaluated by boardgames. Therefore, my raitng is also can be changed after more plays.

RATING: 9
8.0 / 2-5p Best 4 / 100m / 2013 / Overall 35 / Strategy 26 / 3.14
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I dubbed this "Bottleneck the game" after the first turn or two and I think it really fits (in a good way). While the Rondel is completely gone (I don't find the cards similar at all since the penalties/movement restrictions are not there) you do have the same sort of angst here that is present in most (all?) his games where you can see exactly what you want to do, but it will take many many turns to get it done. 

To navigate the bottleneck planning is everything so it's a very strategic game, the game is card driven with your hand of cards (each player starts with exactly the same hand) where each one represents an action so you can plan many many moves ahead here. There are a couple of interesting wrinkles on top of this; you get to add cards to your hand (a little deck building) which not only give you those actions for the future but also it's one of the main ways to score as the cards are multipliers (also similar to some of his other games). This really can create tough choices, because there are some cards you want for their actions yet they may not be the ones you want for scoring. I've said it before and will again, pure deckbuilding bores me to tears, but as just one element in a game it can be really good, and that is the case here. 

Tactics come in via the interaction with the other players in the 2 "market" areas where you can buy cards and where you can pick up resources, and then also in the route-building on the board as areas become more expensive if others get there first (thankfully you are never shut out). The interaction ensures you do need to remain a little flexible in your plans and it can also get you caught up in the tactical grab game, and between that and the bottleneck navigation you can lose sight of the main focus which is maxing out your scoring via collecting cards for their multiplying effect (not just the action) and building on the board accordingly. 

There is very little luck in the game and its another solid offering from Mac. At first I wondered about giving the person who ended the game 7VP, it sounded like a bit of a random bonus but after playing I'm not so sure, it still seems kinda high though maybe 4 or 5 would have been better but its probably fine. I see map is double sided great to see this, and it makes 3 and 4 players both play very good (5 should be fine too just not optimal more PIC, 2 definitely lacking but playable)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Really simple but also really serious game. Every turn you just take one action by playing a card in your hand. You really need to be able to position your cities well so that you can get as much free production from your opponents as possible while not having to give them much. It very vaguely reminds me of another excellent game, Brass, in that regard. To that end the cards that let you produce on all of your cities of a certain resource seem very powerful, since they don't give your opponent anything. Also, it's a game with an interesting timing. Like in Navegador (another Mac Gerts game) the way the game ends means there will be a somewhat leisurely midgame, and then all of a sudden as people start seeing the end in sight they'll start rushing toward it. The scoring is also really cool and makes some of the main decisions of the game more multidimensional. You can supplement your hand with more cards, and the cards have both an action on them and a scoring multiplier. Sometimes the multiplier is slightly at odds with the action and so when choosing cards you have to take both into account and plan accordingly. There is a lot inside this game, definitely my favorite of the KdJ 2014 lot, and I think a perfect fit for the sort of light-medium next level euro that that award is about.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
It's an intriguing game.  You start with a hand of cards that each allow you to make a specific action and once played you can't use it again until you play the Tribune card that allows you to pick up your hand again.  Your settlers race out from the starting city establishing trading houses that will earn you goods that you can then sell for money or use to build more houses, settlers, and improved cards.  Crucially, the cards that you have bought provide your victory points at the end of the game and so you tailor your board strategy to the cards that you are buying.  The rules are pretty light but there is a lot of depth of strategy and there is not a clearly right decision to make each turn so you really have to weigh your options.  To mix things up, there are different boards you can get and the Salt expansion provides some additional flavor with Forum tiles that provide different abilities.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Concordia is an extremely satisfying mix of deck building, area control, and engine building. The start of the game does feel a bit samey each time - players should race to claim cities and prefect to build initial resources - but the game quickly diversifies as players decide which of the point-scoring strategies will be the winner. The gameplay is extremely streamlined without sacrificing strategic (and tactical) depth. I recommend it for seasoned gamers and those looking for the next step beyond gateway games.

RATING: 6
You have a hand of cards that stack up in front of you as you play them, including one card that returns all those previously-played cards to your hand.  You can acquire more cards so you can perform some actions more often before replenishing or so you can perform new types of actions.  On the board, you're moving workers around to build buildings which produce resources.  At the end of the game, each card scores points by category, mostly based on what you built and where.  All of it fits together pretty well, so if it sounds like something you might like, you probably will.  Update: If I played more, I could probably figure out what would make an effective strategy.  As it is, I kind of just flail around for a while and then the game ends. Update2: I had kind of decided to stop playing this, then I accidentally agreed to play it again (as an unnamed heavy euro that I didn't recognize right away). I'm still zigging when I should be zagging and realizing the right move too late.

RATING: 8.5
2014-09-23

Initial Rating: 7.0 (September 2014)

What a pleasant surprise. I heavily dislike Mac Gerdts´ Antike and Imperial series so I wasn’t too keen on playing Concordia. Mercifully it doesn’t have the Gerdts´ characteristic rondel and that’s why it is a good game :P. I only bought it because it was on a sale and now I’m glad I did, for now it is a keeper in my game collection.

Concordia is a medium weight euro game with very interesting/hard decisions each turn (basically which card to play, but each card has far reaching consequences). You want to do many things at once, but you have to choose carefully the few actions you will actually do, lots of good tension. I like it a lot. The end game scoring is great as you chose your path with the cards you bought.

Concordia also have a high replayability factor due to the dual map, the modular city tiles and the bonus goods in each region. Each game will be different. The playtime is around 2 hours but you don’t feel it, also the endgame can arrive very suddenly. 

It has lots of rules, but they are easy to grasp. I feel the game plays best with 4 fast players, with 5 the downtime can be an issue.

The game components are GREAT (excellent wooden bits) and the art is good enough, the game board is very big (which make storage very hard) and it looks gorgeous on the table.

On the bad side, I don’t like the Concordia card, it gives 7 VPs to the player who finish the game I feel it is a bit unbalanced, but I’m not completely sure yet. I have to play more to see if this is an issue for me. The theme is paper thin for me, I still haven’t read the Concordia history book they included in the game but I didn’t felt I was playing on ancient Rome. The middle scoring was a hassle to do, I felt it interrupted the game flow. All the players wanted to continue, but counting the VPs can be slow. I get that it is important for new players to see how the scoring works, but I don’t think I will play with it again.
 
Bottom line, Concordia is a good euro game. It doesn’t really offer anything new, but I like what it offers and how all the pieces work together. Will play again gladly.

Current Rating: 7.0
[hr]
2014-10-02

I continue to like Concordia, now it is a solid 7.0 for me with a high probability of improving. In general I dislike variable market offerings (like in Ascension and Lewis & Clarke) but this game´s market is very well done. It doesn’t bother me because: 1. There are (for most personalities) many repeat cards in function and to what god it prays so getting what I want for my strategy is very likely if I plan for it. 2. There are diplomats that allow me to copy a powerful effect made by another player in case I wasn’t able to buy the card. 3. The cost system is very well done, buying the newest cards is indeed VERY expensive (unlike L&C) so the luck of the deck is minimized. However the Minerva cards can be very powerful in effect and VPs and they are only one of each in the deck. I need to play more to see if this is an issue for me.

Concordia continues to hold my attention with very interesting decisions, it is hard to know the best card to play. I really like it.

After more plays, the Concordia card still bothers me. It decided the winner in our last game and it can be hard to plan to get it, so I feel it is mostly luck if a player gets it or not. Building the fifteen houses can be controlled, but being the last player to buy a card is hard because it depends on other players. I think it would be better if this card doesn’t exist and all the players had the same amount of turns.

RATING: 8
I enjoy this and I think it is a good game. It is challenging piecing together what you need. Sometimes it is challenging figuring out what you need. I think it takes a few plays to grok the connections between your choices and how the scoring ends up. 

The key aspect of this game is that you draft cards to use as actions. Not exactly deck-building, more like hand-building. However, each action card also scores for you in one of six ways at the end of the game - so not only are you picking up actions, you are defining what you will score points for. Understanding the relationship between these when you draft your cards is the biggest challenge here and it is a good one. Mac Gerts has shown that he can successfully move away from the rondel that he made his name on!

No new mechanics here. I found the game rather dry and uneventful because there is no scoring until the end. There is virtually no player interaction leaving me feeling cold. I like the Ancient Roman Empire theme but didn't get the sense I was growing my civilization.

That said, I think the game is solid and there are plenty of opportunities to employ strategy. The problem is for me, I've seen it all before and it comes down to number crunching. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5

A great economic game with a relatively simple rules set.  Every play matters.  When you play your cards (someone may copy your move), when you buy cards(weighting will it be there next turn against making other cards less expensive for your opponents), when/where you move your colonists (will those cities be more expensive next turn). Every play matters, because every play impacts your opponents choices and offers you potential synergies.

One of the biggest synergies in the game are the cards.  The cards you purchase give you spacial abilities, but they also give you end game multipliers.

RATING: 7.5
This is a really nice game of expanding your trade empire, producing goods and collecting cards to give you certain abilities and victory points at the end. You can walk over land or sail. Each round you play a card from your hand to do certain actions. At some point you play a card to bring them all back into your hand. There is a diplomacy card that lets you play off other players cards. There is also some interaction since if you produce in a region this activates the production of other players in the region. Maybe the only weakness is you really have no idea how the other players are doing until the end. This is fine to me. Just enjoy playing your own game and see how it works out in the end.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
An instant modern classic!
I am a big fan of Mac Gerdts's designs, even among them Concordia stands out. It is extremely simple and streamlined yet offers a rich array of strategy and depth. It holds up exceptionally well to repeated plays, and handles 2-5 players very well.

On your turn you play a card and do the action. There are 8 different actions and you have only 6 of these in your opening hand. In order to score points you must build in cities and also acquire action cards (each of which provides end game points for having built cities in certain ways).

The beauty of the game is in the player interaction. You care a lot what other players are doing. If they build in a city it increases the price for you to also build there. If they produce in a province you will gain resources from the cities you've built in that province. If they have meeples occupying spaces you cannot end your turn there, which limits where you can build. And most importantly, they can buy cards from the common card row. 

The game rewards planning and predicting what others are going to do. Add the excellent salsa expansion and one of the other map expansions and you have enough variety to enjoy this game for at least hundreds of plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Even though Concordia doesn't feature a rondel it is clearly recognizable as a Mac Gerdts game by the way players define their endgame scoring themselves. This time, players buy action cards that more importantly count as multipliers for game end scoring in one of six categories (e.g. cities built, provinces settled, number of goods produced). 
The rondel has been replaced with these cards resembling a deck-building mechanism. Instead of random shuffles players have all of them available. Once discarded a player can pick them up again with another card allowing this. As dictated by the VP conditions, players stream out of Rome with their colonists to build new cities that can be used to produce resources to build other cities or buy action/VP cards. The map-based gameplay is a nice element in these worker placement-driven times.
Concordia is a fine game of efficiency in resource conversion. In our game, all four players ended up very close between 109 and 115 VPs. It's to be seen how much possibilities there are for more focussed strategic play instead of our mainly tactical optimizations. In the case of Concordia, the better might turn out to be the enemy of the good with [gameid=66589] filling the same niche and [gameid=24181] providing a richer although different experience.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I really like the way the mechanics work in Concordia. It feels as though the hand management part of the game is about deciding what part of your turn you want to do at what time. I've also noticed that the most successful people in Concordia know what they need and when and don't care if it will help other players. 

Essentially, you have cards that each represent a single action. Together, all those cards make up one complete turn. The truly deep strategy comes from deciding what you will do and when. The diplomat card may be one of the best innovations here. It lets you copy a card from another player, but they have to have just played that card last turn. Sometimes you'll sit there waiting and waiting for someone to play a certain card, but you're possibly ruining your own potential in doing that.

The theme would not come across for me had it not included custom wooden pieces. 

I would also like to note that the instructions in this game are the most elegant I've yet seen. After playing the game twice I assumed that the rule book would be between 12-20 pages. It is 2 pages front and back. It is the same size as Ticket to Ride instructions. That to me is amazing design.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Probably the strongest design of Essen 2013. There's nothing revolutionary about Concordia. It's mostly a design that could've been published in 2001, the only nod to modernity is the hint at deck-building. But in this case the traditional design is the strength, as Concordia avoids the single player optimization spreadsheet syndrome that modern eurogame design is so susceptible to.

The board play is interesting enough, with a somewhat constrained topology and a need to race for the right cities to support the strategy. There's a moderate level of blocking, but for the most part it's of the nuisance variety rather than having the ability to kneecap opponents.

The core idea of the game is the way in which all action cards also function as VP category multipliers, which is clearly an evolution from Navegador's multiplier chips (and a big improvement). So it's not a typical engine building game of cranking up infrastructure first and VPs second. The means of production and the means of affecting the game are also the means of scoring VPs.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
A really clear and easy to grasp, but hard to master card game using different boards as a way to differentiate the setup. On a quick look it looks like a board game about colonizing, but it is rather a handbuilding game and this mechanism gives place for some tough decision making: whether to take your cards back now or trying to utilize the leftovers further and gain some bonus money in the process; and especially which cards to add to your hand because besides of getting cards to support your card play strategy the cards also count as VP multipliers. There's a good level of complexity and very little downtime that's mainly coming from the use of Mercator as it requires some counting as well as the first/second turn Architect optimization. What I also like is the aspect that you have to really keep an eye on what cards others are playing because of the Diplomat. All in all this is deceptively simple, play a card a turn elegance, but it is also very dry.

RATING: 9
I am enjoying this more and more each time I play it (even though I seem to be forgetting to log them all).  I should read the back story, I gather you are setting up some sort of trading empire in Roman times.  I don't actually care what the nitty gritty theme is as it plays very well.

The (semi) random set up of the provinces and the cards coming out in a different order each game means that there is always a slight difference.

Do you go for expensive goods? Do you go for colonists?  Do you go for many provinces? DO you go for different types of goods?  A combination of the above?  Do you set up operations in cities or hire people to assist you?

Decisions... Decisions...

Basically there is a lot going on and you can't do all of it.

RATING: 4
Concordia is a well designed game, but I'm not a fan of these "mini-action" games, where you decide what you are doing next and then spend up to half a dozen turns mechanically working towards that goal. Like you go "I'll build a building there" and then need to spend the next turns getting that done: get resources, trade them for another resources, take money, get building card back to hand, play a building card. Since Concordia is so light on randomness and player interaction, you rarely if ever need to alter your tactical decisions after you've set your mind on one, which makes the game very boring for me especially when combined to it's lengthy playing time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Wow. This game is SOOO GOOD. It totally absorbs you, you lose all track of time, forget to eat and drink, and all the stuff you need to think about, it's almost like programming. And then, at once, it switches from programming to panicky point grabbing for the day of reckoning is near. It has everything I love in a game, and it fits on the table too.
Easily 2 hours game time, but man it has no downtime at all, it's intense and amazing.
The discussion in the forum about point salad? No way! It has so many things to think about, to plan ahead, to make those aggravating choices letting go of some of those great goals. There is nothing point salad about this.

RATING: 6
In Concordia, Gerdts recycles mechanics from his earlier games in a slightly different package. The rondel is replaced by personal action selection cards, that you can buy more with resources, and they also provide scoring multipliers in the end. They work in a very similar way the rondel does.

No match for Imperial in any way. Scoring needs some planning, which is nice. Nothing really new or novel here, but I'll happily try again.

RATING: 8.5
Gasp! a Mac Gerdts game without a roundel! =)  A nice deep game that combines hand building, map settling and various interlocking scoring metrics.  I like the choices, the far reaching effects of decisions, the limited but significant interaction with other players, etc.  On the downside, it's generally unclear who's leading and why throughout the game.  You have suspicions but they are just that.  The velocity also keeps ramping up and generally leads to a very abrupt end of play.  Then you count points, and you are sometimes surprised by who the winner is.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
It's a typical average euro game where nothing spectacular actually jump out of it. The action system with cards is interesting on a first glance because you get rewarded in gold if you managed to use many of them before taking your cards back. That is something I would be seeing in other games.

But the problem is the lack of structure. Instead of having a fixed number of actions, not only 1 action has 2 copies but you can also buy additional cards which increases your hand of cards making it more and more annoying to plan ahead your actions.

Personally, I would have made the new cards you bought replace the older ones so that you always have the same nb of actions you can use. There is also a lack of consistency in the actions cards because some are more powerful, but some other are the same. So why should I buy higher level cards if they do not bring me something new.

Also regarding those cards, it affect the way you will score points. Now this could get pretty confusing because you must first check if you can afford the cards, then check if it gives you an action you want and finally check if it will make you score a lot of points at the end, which you currently don't know. Maybe at the end of the game, there could be more reason to check for VP instead of the action since you might not have a chance to use it.

Finally, one of the thing that slightly annoyed me is that to place a house on a city that produce resource X, you need resource X. Well guess what, If I do want a city that produce X, is because I do not have any X resources. So you'll need to find other ways to get the resource.

Also buying and selling is pretty limited. I would have liked to be able to buy and sell more than 2 resource per turn. It would give me a reason to produce resources and sell them afterward. Else, I produce resource, it fills up my warehouse and I get stuck with it. So not very joyful.

I don't think it's a bad game, if you like euros, you could like this game, but it personally does not catch me at all.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Juego buenisisisimoooooooooooooo de lo mejor que he jugado
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I love the interaction of this game and the card system + board tactics are both very interesting.  Scoring and strategy is not easy to grasp in your first game.  I think I like many of these mechanisms that go together fairly well (creates a lot of interaction with direct confrontation) and it's not an offensive game at all.  

This Euro is all about synergizing your "end game scoring strategy" with accumulating the right multipliers (cards) to make your strategy payoff big.  I think it rewards players who can build enough on the board tactically while find enough good cards that match what they're doing on the board.  If one accumulates too many cards, it might not help.  Also, like many Euro games, pursuing things which other people are not directly doing is advantageous usually.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
FIRST IMPRESSION:

I must admit I was ignoring this title as I generally don't enjoy Mac Gerdt's titles but this one is okay...I won't run away screaming and there isn't an established rondel that must be used although one could argue the cards form a different type of rondel.  There's a pleasant balance to be found between obtaining additional cards to assist with scoring/additional actions and building cities.  I'd be open to playing this one again.  My first play was a bit disjointed due to the smallish print on the cards but as you become familiar with the cards, the text becomes largely irrelevant.  

========================================

Upped my rating to a 7 after my second play.  I'm finding this one more interesting and it may be slowly entering the buy zone.  My main concern with the game is that there can be considerable AP at times when one has all their cards in hand...

========================================

Keeping my rating at a 7 but sold my copy. This game takes about 30 minutes longer with my group than I prefer. Still happy to play from time to time, but can play someone else's copy.

RATING: 7
The game does not give obvious goals at the beginning: there are many options to follow. I prefer to have clearer incentives, as this gives some nice clarity. 

I am not a big fan of the point salad at the end: it is hard to know why you win or why you lose.

The game ends up being an exercise in organization and optmization… unfortunately like many other euros. 

A key to winning might be to count enough to value of cards so as to better see needs and opportunities. 

Otherwise, the rules are clear and simple enough (we missed a few rules: two *types* of goods, moves by multiple stems). 

Nice material. Nice map. The card powers are simple enough.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I like this game a lot.  I struggle with balancing in-game objectives with card-collecting so that I can really maximize end-game scoring (which is basically just game scoring).  If you can really wrap your head around the fact that all your city buying and goods-trading/selling is really only good if you have a decent collection of cards for each objective, you can have a lot of fun with this game and score A LOT of points!

RATING: 6
I admit it: Upon viewing this game, with its resources, its map of the Mediterranean, its "whatever" title, its box cover of Some White Person With a Boat in the Background, I was not expecting much out of it. But I ended up liking it a bunch. It looks more overwrought than it is, and ability to plan your moves and your scoring without a bunch of ridiculous "but don't neglect THIS, or else you can't win!" nonsense tacked on to ensure you visit every area of the game OR ELSE was refreshing. There's still a little Eurojunk rattling around, such as restrictions on buying new cards that seem to exist solely to give the opportunity to have a card that avoids them, but otherwise it's a nice step back from the current trend of piling everything at arm's reach into a game and then hoping that interesting play emerges. That said, I'm not certain there are more than a handful of plays in this.

RATING: 7
Charming game for some reasons, disappointing for some others. Kind of a summary of all the twists that made Mac Gerdts' games so appreciated, beautiful and top quality materials and incredibly simple and elegant rules.
With that being said, what makes me like this game way less than other Euros in my collection (including other Gerdts' games) is that while you play you feel that it's something already done, something already seen. 
Plus, its most original element, i.e. the scoring phase at the end of the game, makes me puzzled; during my games I can hardly manage what's the situation at the other ends of the table, so I try to do the maximum points and thus postpone the end of the game, often doing a favor to other players. 
Maybe it's just me, but it feels like Concordia is not a very governable game in terms of scoring.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
If it wasn't that so many people were enthusiastic about this game, then i wouldn't have played it. Because the box nor the board, look inviting. 

But now that i've made that effort, I do understand the enthusiasm. This is a fast playing game with lots of ways to score points, different strategies to win. 

Playing one of your cards each turn, is something that everyone should be able to do. But of course, the order that you play them , and the ones you add to your deck, makes the difference.

Not sure yet about replay ability. You could say, its just a "multiplier collection" game, but it doesn't really feel that way.

If I need to say something negative, it's play length and down time with 5 players. 
Another downside, is that during the game, it's difficult to get an idea about who is winning. That has a negative influence on game tension. 

Minus: 2 - 5

RATING: 5

I played this game to test whether I really do not like low-interaction, many-paths-to-victory games, and I think it is now confirmed. The reason is that I completely see the greatness of Concordia, as it's smooth to play, relatively simple, has cute little bits, there's tough hand management, and you have to carefully plan ahead... but it just did not interest me, as for me it boils down to making an engine for resource conversion. If you like that kind of game, you will probably love Concordia, and I wouldn't hesitate to recommend it to you... but it's just not for me.

RATING: 9.75
I'm playing this pretty much any time I get the chance at the moment, I really like it. That's probably somewhat affected by my above the norm (for me) success rate - something I expect will fall off as I play with more experienced players.

I like Mac Gerts games and I like rondels (even pseudo rondels like here). This one is very clever because although it's 'play a card, take the action, next player' what you can do on your turn can be a hard choice. Similarly, it's a set-collecting game, but the nuance is in exactly which sets to collect. Currently the dominant strategy seems to be Jupiter/Saturn with either Mercury or Minerva to supplement. Hopefully we'll see some evolution in future games, though letting go of Jupiter or Saturn will cede it to others who will gain huge benefit.

On reflection after a *lot* of games on BaJ, I've come to the conclusion that the Minerva strategy is important, rather than a supplement. To the extent that if anyone gets hold of one more Minerva than everyone else, that person will likely win; getting Vintner and Cloth Merchant is a killer combo.

RATING: 9.5
I was originally put off by the eponymous "Trading the Mediterranean Theme" but the buzz got me interested. 

A great game of efficiency.  Newer players quickly discover that it is a mistake to try and do everything.  The best play is to try and play to certain gods that you are trying to collect (and, of course, try to collect gods that no-one else is going for).  Streamlined card play make turns quick, particularly for a slightly heavier strategic game.  There are interesting decisions as you face a trade-off between efficiency and tempo.  I definitely recommend everyone try this game at least once.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Concordia feels like a throwback euro with some modern additions. The rules are quite simple (4-page rulebook), yet there are a large number of strategic and tactical decisions to make. One can come up with an outline of a strategy based on the random setup of goods and cards, but will need to deviate based on other players' actions. The emerging chaos in the game feels player driven as opposed to many modern euros in which you seem to be competing against the game itself. Determining the order in which to execute your card actions (as well as when to pick up your deck and start over) is fascinating as is determining which cards to purchase. The game offers 5-6 ways in which to score points and each card acts as a multiplier for one of the scoring categories in addition to building your deck and offering in-game actions. The game plays in 90 minutes with 3-4 players and runs very smoothly as each player plays a single card per turn.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Really enjoy this with any player count. Very nice mix of deck building, hand management and resource management. You also have a little area control in the sense that it is cheaper to build first in a production area, so early game "blocking" is possible. 

Very simple gameplay (choose a card from your hand, play the card, do the action) but choices can be agonizing - as with many good games, it feels like everything you do is one step behind where you want to be. There can be downtime waiting for your turn, but I use that to plan, especially as things you think you want to do might go away as others take their turn and you have to quickly re-plan. Even when many players are new, they get into a rhythm of planning and executing, so turns progressively get faster. Production quality is excellent - except for the horrid box cover art...

RATING: 6
Concordia is a very well designed game that, despite focusing on resource management and conversion, manages to feel different from all the other games that do the same thing. One huge divergence is that that there's no tired worker placement; instead there's a clever mix of card drafting, hand management, and navigating a board whose setup changes with every game. Like other games of its ilk, Concordia creates some pretty clear boundaries for effective play: sure you can [i]try[/i] to build only houses at the expense of getting cards, but you won't win...and vice versa. But within these bounds, there's still a lot of variability for how you can approach the game. Some cards might combine well with others or how the board is laid out; it's up to you to notice these opportunities and pounce on them before others do. But you might pay a steep price to get exactly what you want, creating opportunities for others to carve a strategy from the unappreciated cards and provinces at a discount. All in all, I definitely can see myself getting at least a few plays out of this. Solid.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Spiel 2015

Although this game is very recent, it feels like a classic Eurogame in which the mechanics work perfectly together... Rule-wise, it's quite easy (rules fit on 4 pages including examples), but strategically, it's at the high end! An excellent game with a good flow, rules are very easy but mastering the game is something else because there are a lot of things that you need to consider. Buying cards is crucial in order to get points, but don't forget about the board as without settlements, you don't get points either. Only downside is that scores are only calculated at the very end of the game so you cannot easily see who's in the lead and that the game is not very forgiving.

RATING: 7.8
Played this at an open games meet-up and really quite enjoyed it. The game was a bit slow for the first half but as it was played by four new players (myself included) and one explainer who hadn’t played much himself this wasn’t too surprising. As we got our heads around the mechanics the gameplay sped up and people started thinking about strategy rather than rules which, for mine, is always a good sign in a game.

I liked how the game played, there are multiple paths to victory, interesting ways that players can make life difficult for their opponents as well as genuine and challenging decisions every turn about what role to use and where to concentrate ones efforts. This is complimented by high-quality components and attractive artwork that really compliment the Roman theme. From my one play I suspect that this game has great replayability.

In the game I played the first four players ended up all within five or six points of each other despite all having very different approaches. I would definitely play this game again and would consider for purchase if the price was right.

RATING: 7
I'm a bit ambivalent about Concordia. On one hand, I really like the action mechanic, where your actions are represented by cards and that you choose in which order to play them. Your goal is to expand your trade network but you will constantly be faced with challenges like "I need a wheat market to produce wheat but I need wheat to build a wheat market". There is also a good balance between accomplishing good things on the map and buying new action cards that earns you victory points for how well you did on the map.

On the other hand, the action cards you buy don't give you new or better options, just more of the same, so the gameplay changes little from action card cycle to cycle and the game feels slightly too long. Also, the lack of in-game scoring makes the end-game scoring too long.

Concordia is still a great game but I think similar games like Hansa Teutonica and Lancaster has some tension and variety during the game.

RATING: 7
In ancient Rome, each player starts with an identical hand of 7 cards. Cards allow one to collect income, move colonists, build cities, and buy other cards. Each card is also worth game ending VPs. Points are scored in a variety of ways: diversity of cities in provices, diversity in different types of cities, number of colonists and so forth.

There is no Rondel, but you could think of the cards as the rondel. There is a card that lets you pick up all your played cards, so it is similar. There's lots to think about, and lot of tension about who gets to the card display first. 

Whoever triggers the game end gets a 7 VP bonus. I've been victimized by this a couple of times, and also I've gotten the 7 VP bonus and lost. I assume the designer has gone over the playtesting to determine that 7 VP is the right award - it just seems a bit unscientific to me and depending on turn order, you could be deprived one turn relative to other people. So there is something about trying to equalize the turn order that nags at me, but it's an interesting game.

Overall didnt click for me well enough. I think its because its hard to wrap your head around the scoring and the cards were more rehashes of the same thing, just in increased power or combinations. A game with semi smilar scoring that I think works better is Orleans. The "multiuse" of the cards is also a bit of a misnomer as there is usually only one active effect and just a passive scoring one so you can only "use" it during the gameplay for one thing usually.

RATING: 9
Concordia has very elegant, beautiful and easy to teach rules offering a deep game experience at the same time.

Very replayable: different situations in each game. Very short waiting time (one move for player, one after the other, as most of the games by its designer). It is easy to concentrate on the game (not fiddly at all). Very beautiful material, both the map and the wooden pieces. It offers both strategy and tactics. 

The only thing that stops me giving it a ten is that the choices are somehow interesting, but the effect of choosing one path instead of another is not dramatic, very few points are at stage. This allows for a well-balanced game, but a bit less exciting and tense to play. But it is close to being a ten and I can possibly increase the rating in the future.

It has the further advantage that it is a game for all occasions, since it can be taught in a few minutes, it is not long  and it scales very well (it can be played from 2 to 5 players). 

Recommended.

RATING: 6
Mechanically, Concordia isn't a bad game, but it has too many flaws to be worth buying.

For one thing, inexperience with Concordia can lead to poor early moves that will affect a player for the rest of the game, leading that player to feel like there is no hope for victory very early on.

Scarcity of resources can cause turns to be wasted due to not being able to afford any decent actions. 

Most negatively, potential for actions you are wanting to take being taken by other players before your chance negates the usefulness of planning ahead. Combined with a lack of player interaction means players don't even pay attention to the turns of others, wait for their turn to come around, and only then assess the current situation before executing an action. This leads to Analysis Paralysis, long turns, and people getting bored between their own turns and leaving the table or playing with their phones.

Concordia has decent components, a decent board layout, and is not really a bad game, but all its flaws outweigh any positives the game may have.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
A Gerdts with no rondel, didn't think I would see the day. Concordia reminded me a great deal of Trajan, for the fact that this is a game of using various methods to acquire victory points, and there is no punishment or threat of losing VPs. The simple gameplay mechanism of "play a card each turn" is very elegant and to be commended. The learning curve is steep, because interplay between the cards is complex (reminiscent of Lewis & Clark, but with fewer cards), and the scoring is not entirely intuitive (and yet, very similar to Stone Age -- cards act as multipliers to some other criteria in the game).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Card driven rondel game with ability to purchase new cards that become part of the rondel. In the game you play  different cards from your hand. The basic cards allow: your colonists to move around a map and build production centers; produce goods; buy or sell goods for money; buy cards with goods; copy someone's action; take your played cards back into your hand with a possibility to introduce a new colonist.
At game end all purchased cards score points. there are cards that score: all your colonists; production centers dependent on the good type; production centers in different provinces etc.
The game is well put together, but just like most rondel games, feels repetitive, there is a cycle of building, producing, trading with occasional card purchases. I was a little bored, hence the lower rating.

RATING: 8.45

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Medium lenght and complexity economic game featuring a very atttractive cdeck building mechanism. The whole game revolves about it:
-> You just do one action per turn, the action marked by the card you play.
-> These cards also state the ammount of points you will win at the end.

Consequently, the game goes on very fast (barring AP prone players). There are many things you can do, and many moments to do them. The challenge is to choose the right ones, and do them in the right moment.

Those actions also have a physical reflection on a map, and that's where the interaction with the other players will happen (and in the market of available cards, and the exploitation of the regions).

The rules are simple and elegant, making it a game that will be loved by many people, not exclusively by gamers.

The experience is different depending on the number of players and although there are not a lot of variable elements (just the available cards), the changes they produce in the game are huge.

I really love the game classic aesthetics (once I'm past beyong its the rather dull cover).

It's become my favourite Mac Gerdts game!

RATING: 9
Like nearly all Mac Gerdts games, Concordia doesn't really offer anything new. At least there's no rondel this time around, but there is a personal card-stack system adapted from Castle for all Seasons, although it can't be denied that having each card contribute to end-game scoring is a sweet touch.

Concordia ultimately wins its spurs by being tense, competitive, easy to teach and unpredictable: essentially the same great traits as nearly all its progenitors.

RATING: 8
An excellent ancient business building game. The mechanics are so smooth too. 4/3/2014

Verdict:

What I Like: Yes former self, these mechanics are smooth. Mechanically everything makes perfect sense and fits together like a glove (theme you ask? unimportant. not a negative). The game has very low rules overhead but is satisfyingly deep. Deck building to gain more action availability as well as endgame point multipliers is a fascinating addition. Cards drive board play which in turn informs card worth, balance between the two is key. I like that a players control of game length is important to strategy, and that everyone will often avoid good moves as long as possible to deny their opponents great ones. Games of brinksmanship are common here and quite fun.

What I Don't Like: The endgame scoring is somewhat blind. I can usually guess approximately how many points my opponents have, but not always. Scoring itself can be unintuitive for new players.

RATING: 10
[b]Concordia[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (10):[/COLOR]

[i]Concordia is a game that instantly became a favorite after my first play. It offers a such smooth play experience and elegant rule set. The rules are intuitive, everything makes sense (you'll never look up rules while playing). You can teach this game in minutes, but the true beauty of the game is the depth of strategy found in Concordia. Add in the Britannia expansion map and you have an very tight 2p map (which solves the base game's only flaw). I think Concordia will become a classic that is still being played 10 years from now.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]- I love the decision space in this game, planning out your colony expansion while managing your resources and income [i](pure Euro goodness)[/i].
- Plays great with 2; I just ordered the expansion map which should make the 2p experience even better.
- Card management; not only do the cards in your hand provide you with the actions you can take, they also have a scoring rule attached to them. You only score points based on the cards you collect.
- Low rules overhead, this is a very clean design.
(Once you start playing you have a card that lists the scoring opportunities and cost for establishing cities and that is about all yo need to reference while playing.)

I appreciate how focused this design is, the challenge lies in how you play the game in the confines of a very simple and elegant rule set, not in wrestling with a complex rule set full of exceptions and special circumstances. I'm hopeful that this game will see double digit plays by this time next year (I could easily see it becoming one of my favorites).[/i]

5 plays -> Britannia map
2 plays -> Hispania map with Salsa expansion

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
心得:
有趣的規畫遊戲
主要分數來源事實上是牌跟建築
外交官行動的設計非常棒
雖然主要行動可能都是移動然後蓋房子
產資源蓋房子
但是不會讓人感覺很乾
因為卡片計分目標會讓人有方向
還算不錯
規則:
1.根據地圖英文字母隨機放城市版塊，在根據省份的最貴資源放紅利資源版塊於省分區
2.根據人數選用卡片(兩人就用到II，三人用到III)，按數字洗好疊在一起，I在最上面
3.每個人拿起始資源，按順序拿錢從五塊遞增，大長官卡給尾家
4.主要功能卡如下
A 護民官 收卡，超過三張(+這張)每多一張拿一塊，可花資源生工人在羅馬(一個)
B 建築師 幾個人總共移幾步，站在線上，兩端付錢可蓋建築物，每多一棟別人的建築物，付的金錢要乘上去，金錢基底看建築物資源種類1~5塊錢
C 長官 選一個省份紅利版塊翻面得資源，同省份有建築的也可拿資源 或 選拿錢
D 殖民者  放置工人於自己的城市裡 或 拿五塊+工人數的錢
E 莫卡托 拿三塊錢，之後可做兩類物品交易
F 外交官 複製別人行動
G 議員 可買最多兩張牌，要付版圖上的資源
H 執政官  最多買一張，只要付卡上費用
I 其它 收城市資源
5. 遊戲結束照記分卡記分，首次玩可以用中途記分，分數最高兩塊錢，第二一塊錢，記玩分數退回零分
6.變體規則起始無資源，照順位25塊錢遞增，第一回合可任意買資源

RATING: 8.5
At first glance, Concordia seems like a 4x game set in Rome but you'd be wrong. Instead, it relies on predicting what goals your opponents are pursuing and outpacing them. The central mechanic of the game is card-drafting/hand management which takes the sting out of the territory control. There is no attacking or blocking in this game but there is beating your opponents to the punch a split second before they get the card they want. Plus, I love any game where there are multiple strategies to victory. Constantly engaging, turns are swift and are only one action, Concordia is a peaceful civilization game I'll play again and again. Can't wait to try the various maps!

RATING: 8
All the stuff I like in a Euro - no round based stuff, a good tactical decision each turn, a strategy that develops based on the map and card availability that helps drive the tactics, indirect pussage (there being a race for resource generators and cards). It uses the card play/pickup system from Havana / Castle for all Seasons, but I like how when you buy more cards, they add to your hand to keep things going. I also like how you're rewarded for using as many cards as possible before pickup. There's a nice variety of ways to go about scoring points. You want to pay attention to what other people are doing to potentially leech off their card this turn, and also to direct your strategy to alternate ways to score points to minimise direct competition. I was engaged throughout, nice components, nice timeframe, potentially a touch long, and all requiring only 4 pages of rules + setup. An elegant system.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wonderful and Delightful Game! A lot going on, but many simple ideas keep it from melting the brain. "On your turn, play one card and do what it says." But that simple instruction has so many choices, without being overwhelming. Breath of fresh air. NOT multiplayer solitaire. Very little luck, and a good blend of tactics with strategy. Good with 2 players, but better with more.

Warning: AP alert, and much math involved in the scoring. Also, the resource costs are going to be a stumbling block for new players.

RATING: 7.5
The mechanics in this game are very well thought out. The theme of course is just added on but the game play is great. You move your units around to build cities and collect resources with the end goal of collecting scoring cards which are also used to perform actions. There are several ways to score so you can focus on one type and multiply or spread out. Lots of player interaction also as you can block paths, everyone is collecting the same set of cards and production is shared by region. At 5 it gets expensive to build out so the game feels slower and you need to adapt your strategy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
In Concordia, you're trying to build up your network of goods-producing cities, produce goods, and claim cards that will give you end-game multipliers.  It's all based around a hand of cards that can be played in any order, determining your action for the round.  The cards not only list an action, but an end-game multiplier, so acquiring new cards has multiple purposes.  The effectiveness of your actions is highly dependent on what other people are doing, making this a very tactical game.  Because the scoring multipliers are all in people's hands, it's often difficult to figure out how people are doing while the game is in progress.

(5+ plays)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Rules are incredibly simple, gameplay is quick and you do have some agonizing decisions during the game. The quality of the wooden pieces and the board is great. I wish the cards were linen; sleeved, the cards are just fine. I rate it an 8, but it borders a 9. I don't think it has enough "umph" though to push it there. Solid game, but not an amazing one.

Only randomness is the setup and the order in which the purchasable cards come out. Doesn't seem like much, but that provides just enough randomness to keep it fresh. I don't see this getting a TON of play, but I feel like it will be fun for 20-30 plays before it needs to be shelved for a while.

RATING: 9
I love this game. In no particular order, 10 things I love:
1. Quick to learn. Short rule book! Intuitive to play.
2. It is a game of abundance, not famine.
3. There are many great options to choose from each turn, rather than a feeling of needing to identify 1 ideal move.
4. There are several ways to get the things you want; and if you still don't like those options, you can pivot your strategy and focus on getting something else.
5. Scoring happens at the END of the game, so you can remain engaged and optimistic about your chances throughout the game.
6. The replay-ability looks to be great.
7. I like the 90-minute play time. Feels not short and not long. 
8. Plays well with 2!
9. The game is beautiful on the table.
10. Even though the artwork on the box cover is not great, I still appreciate that it's a woman on the cover who isn't over-sexualized.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Well, Concordia is just a great game about establishing trade houses and trading resources in ancient times. Seems dry? It is. But Concordia is a deceptively simple, very elegant game with deep gameplay. Opening the box and seeing just 4 pages of rules is a nice surprise indeed. It has a deck-building mechanism for action selection which sets it apart from it's competitors gameplay wise. Only negative is that the scoring (the most important part, I might add!) takes a few plays to get the hang of, and you have to be aware of what cards (the scoring is based on the cards) your opponents take from the ones on offer, not paying attention will often lose you the game. A definite keeper, glad it came back in print. Unfortunately does not work as well as a 2 player for us though, a bit too open and without a crowded map, the excitement kind of disappears, but we still play it 2 from time to time.

RATING: 8
really liked this one. the cards you buy to get more actions also determine what gives you points, and to get the cards you need resources, which you get by spreading out on the board (trying to be first, because that's cheaper, but you can always get in), and spreading out is easier if you invest in more dudes to have travel around the map. And there's a nice way that production works, where an area becomes unavailable for production after it has produced once, meaning that there are fewer and fewer areas that can still produce resources, until someone picks the companion action to producing and makes all areas available again (netting them money).

RATING: 7
I got to play this at my local meetup today. I was very excited to give this game a try since it was on my wish list.

This game is heavy in a way that you don't know what you want to do. In the beginning the strategy isn't all that clear and the scoring is very opaque. I have no idea who is in the lead and what moves can get me the most points. That leaves me with the core mechanism of the game, which is a rondel with cards. At the end of the game besides the initial cards that we start with, we only ended up getting 3~4 more cards for the new player and the experienced player at 4~6 cards. 

Cards are extremely important because they provide end game scoring. It feels like the game you build up and build up and try to grab cards if you can and you really need to manage your actions well in order to do good or you will always be one step behind on what you would like to do. It's a pure Euro through and through. Try to maximize your production engine while not waste time over producing (wasting) it since space is limited.

I got a bad case of Analysis Paralysis sometimes in the game and so did my playmates. Even we have all the time in the world to plan it still bums us out when some place got taken or some resources being blocked from others.

The diplomat card (copy someone's last played card) is super important in this game. I like this mechanism though.

RATING: 8.5
Only 2 plays so far, but I like it a LOT. And yes, the Britannia expansion is wonderful for 2p because it forces head-to-head competition. Granted, the "competition" isn't anywhere near as fierce as one might think. Paying 2 coins to build where your competitor has a presence turns out to be a minimal cost. Our first game we avoided each other like a plague and the play was odd. But in the second play we tried competing for settlements and the lightbulb came on. Getting somewhere first isn't about blocking an opponent, but merely a way of generating money for further expansion. As long as both players lead in at least one critical area, the money flows back and forth causing a bit of "feast or famine" which ebbs and flows and overlaps with the ebb and flow of the deck as you use it and renew it. Balancing deck construction, deck usage, expansion, and revenue from various sources (including 1st-settler premiums from opponents) is so cool. 

Great game... quite a bit of strategy is involved in this relatively mid-weight game.

RATING: 7
I finally got a chance to play this, and I'm not quite believing the hype. This is a "there's a lot to do and you can't do everything"-type of game. But it feels like you can't do anything well, just do a bunch of stuff for points and then the game ends. Sure, there are different strategies and different aspects to focus on, but no real engine-building or surprising moves or anything like that.  There are also a lot of constraints: you can't hoard goods, you can only play a certain card so many times, to build a city to gain a good you already need to have that good, getting cards early is prohibitively expensive, you can't dominate a region since you're rewarded for spreading out and leeching from others and so on. It's fine-tuning and grabbing points when the opportunity presents itself, which makes for a fine, but unexciting game.

RATING: N/A
P: 2-5, T: 100 min, KenSdJ-N
Peaceful expansion and economic development (Euro style interactions). Card drafting/deck building to take actions; clever mechanic to decide when to recall your deck (rather than just as it runs out like most games). Card drafting simultaneously provides lots of scoring options (set collecting).  After random board seeding (replay variability), luck influences very little. Scales well with two maps for higher or lower player counts.

Medium weight; simpler rules than you might expect, probably not a gateway but not terribly difficult to teach. Fairly classic, but emotionless/thinly themed Euro (blocking, resource management, puzzly, number crunching)

Remind me a bit of Stone Age (different resources, civilization cards that give tangible benefit and end game scoring, various viable strategic approaches) + Power Grid (area control, more expensive to move into occupied regions)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
An intricate euro with a lot of interesting interlocking systems. The individual systems present interesting decisions to make: which general strategy to pursue, whether to pursue your long-term goals or pounce on a juicy short-term opportunity, which card role to use, when to refill your hand, whether to take resources or money, how your action will affect other players and vice versa, etc. Those elements would be good by themselves, but they all work together very smoothly to make fascinating decisions almost every turn. I like that most of the actions you take are pretty generous as well. I rarely feel like "I have to take this action to prevent my engine from withering or my people from starving". Instead, most turns are "These two options would really help me out in different ways. Which one do I do? Or should I take this slightly worse option because it will hamper other players?".

The only real downside is that the theme and presentation are bland and flavorless. This is the standard Euro theme of "Trading in the Mediterranean", which is so overdone that even jokes about it feel tired. Even so, the theme is strong enough to bind the gameplay elements together, so if the players are more interested in tough decisions and elegant interactions than theme, the game is excellent.

RATING: 8.5
A great euro that really revolves around this novel hand management mechanic. The decisions of when to play which card involve plenty of strategy. There's also a bit of area control as well as resource management with the colonization and building. Money is also incredibly tight in the early phases of this game. The only issue I have for this game is that the scoring is hidden. It's hard to tell who is ahead and you have to be counting who has bought which cards, which will dictate scoring. This makes it very difficult to avoid helping the person who is ahead. There is a run for the cards at the end of the game when the engines are built up and players can afford cards, and that is typically how the game ends.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Procedurally a rather simple game, on your turn play a card do what it says. 
The card roles allow you to produce goods in a province or collect money, Move and build, buy/sell goods, buy more cards (roles to choose from), copy last action by another player, finally collect your cards played cards and perhaps get a small bonus and deploy a new builder. The map is important since building after others cost a factor more and the builders can block access to a city site. It is the players order of selection that adds uncertainty not the the draw.

The scoring is the most complicated part but adds a whole layer of complexity. Each card has a god on the bottom. These don't affect game play, but act as multipliers for scoring. So you are looking at not only what the action the card provides but will the god multiplier helps your scoring. Specialization can pay off quite a bit. 

Not a hard game to get started, but you find yourself frequently wondering how to best improve your position in your chosen path.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Concordia is a great game. It's fairly simple, plays in around 90 minutes, but is packed with tense decisions. Games can play out quite differently due to the random setup with various locations being spread across the board and the way the new cards come out. The scoring is also varied enough such that you can really explore many different paths for getting lots of points. The turns are interesting as you can save up for some massive turns by setting up for them. It's a game that clearly rewards good long term planning while also rewarding those players that can be a bit flexible to take advantage of the changes in game state made by the other players. Interaction is there, although that is perhaps one of the weaker parts as for the most part everyone is making their own plans and you won't get too screwed over by what others do--but occasionally you can be and it can make a large difference in the outcome. 

Overall I came away quite impressed by my first play and could see this rising in rating as repeated plays reveal more of the strategic depth I believe to be there.

RATING: 8
The more I play it, the more I like it. Seems like a very simple eurogame at first (and the rules ARE simple), but there's so much goodness hidden in the game's dynamics... There's no direct interaction here - or at least no confrontation - but how people play affects everyone around. For starters, you really need to be able to assess the tempo of the game, because card value can change depending on how fast people buy cards from the track. 

But that's just the tip of an iceberg - there are also different dillemas here: build close to others or far away? When to use the Diplomat card? Use Architect often for small profit or maximize his action to build a lot in one turn? So much to think about! Quite an unique game in that regard - deeper than you might think after your first play.

Still, I wouldn't call it a deck-building game, as this element is minor. Sure, you buy cards, but often not to build an efficient deck - rather to get the right scoring cards. I recommend this highly, a game that offers a lot more than it seems after scanning the rules or even playing for the first time. Personal note: works a lot bettter with 4, as a 2-player game it was only decent.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
Another Euro with quite good mechanics but a very dry theme. It is somehow innovative that you build your hand of possible actions that at the same time are your victory point generators. This game is probably very well balanced, since all player were in the same victory point range (max. difference of 10), even though rather different strategies were employed.

I personally do not like the game that much, because I get  turned of by the aspect of time in this game (same is  true for Gerdt's rondell games): I might have much more resource generating cities than others, but every city (actually region) has to be activated to produce. So having less cities which are activated the same number  of times gives you the same output. I rather prefer an automatic economy with a certain output in a certain time frame.

The art work and the map are not really inviting (especially the cover!). Some symbols are  really hard to recognize (price on cards). Why the cards must have a brownish-grey background (bad contrast to text) is probably only known to the graphic designer.
