RATING: 10
I'm fairly new to this game, but it's quickly becoming an obsession.

This game gives you quite a bit of freedom, while restricting that freedom just enough. 

It's as if the designer hired a group of psychologists to analyze the most powerful psychological reinforcers in board games. You get the satisfaction of spreading your empire over a map and building it in the way that you choose.You simultaneously feel powerful and rich while never feeling like you quite have enough to do everything you hope to do.

Own the Concordia: Salsa expansion along with the Britannia/Germania and the Gallia/Corsica maps.

RATING: 7
(+) Turns go by smoothly. The play-a-card-perform-an-action adage often attributed to this game rings true. 
(+) There is great depth in the assembly of your pool of cards, and the action selection plan which you set out to do on a sequence of turns. The Diplomat is especially powerful, as it allows you flexibility you would not normally have, letting you leech off of others' more advanced card purchases.
(+) Despite its simple turn structure, this game is rich with strategy. It achieves this without being bogged down with overly convoluted mechanisms.

(-) Lacking in player interaction.
(-) The game feels 'peaceful' or 'thoughtful', and does not ever incite feelings of excitement.
(-) The scoring system is questionable; worried that it is a case of whosoever bought the most cards is the winner. This is likely more prevalent at lower player counts.

RATING: 8.5
i wish i craved to play this game as often as i want to play Power Grid. such a solid game of spreading out with surprisingly little blocking (and no random "take-that" steals, skips, etc). my two minor hold-ups with this game would be a.) i'm a little tired of deckbuilders and that carries over to this game even if it's more of a hand-builder with no shuffling, and b.) missing out on card purchases can be devastating even if you're crushing it on the city-building front (ask my wife!). having said that, wow i enjoy this game! the smaller maps are a great addition, by the way. i look forward to playing through every map i own and roping in non-Pandemic double-date game nights. this is definitely the kind of game i'm thinking of when i imagine downsizing my collection and having only the best of the best (for us) on the shelf.

RATING: 9
Surprisingly simple clear minimalist rules and very interesting gameplay. Planning and execution is important for playing well; there is almost no randomness, except for the relatively minor randomness of the order that later cards come out to be available for purchase via Senator action. Neat tension between building more cities on the board and acquiring cards; you need some of both. I really like the central mechanism of playing one card from your hand for its action, and one of the cards is Tribune to take back all the cards you've played.

We've mostly been playing 2-player on the base game's Italy map and enjoying it a lot.

I see many people say the Salsa expansion improves it; for me, the base game is already very interesting and cool and clever!

The box is a bit oddly large and unusually proportioned. On the other hand, it's similar in length and width to Power Grid, which we own and like very much.

If my wife wanted to own it, I'd happily own it; but as it currently stands we have a friend who loans us his set sometimes. :)

RATING: 7.5
Feels a little bit like a more complex Thurn and Taxis for some reason, where you're deciding what towns you're going to go for and the most efficient way to build them, although mechanically it's totally different.  I really like the variable board set up, where certain resources may be in short supply or concentrated in certain sections on the map, and then they interact with the resource you get when you activate that province (so a resource that is in short supply on the board but is present in the province slots will get activated more often). I kept wishing there were more of the specialist cards in the deck (there's just more of each).  I can see this easily modified with expansions for more variation in the role cards.  I kind of liked how the rules had a beginner rule for intermediate scoring - it has no impact on the game, but rather is just a way for new players to be reminded of how endgame scoring will work.

RATING: N/A
3 player, 4 player, 5 player
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Concordia is not much to look at based on the box cover and board... but do not let this one pass you by. Players play members of the ancient Roman empire trying to expand their influence in the empire at the same time gaining favor with the Roman gods. Players have a starting deck of cards and players use those cards for actions on the board and players have the option of expanding their deck with more  card purchases. The point values of the items on the board depend on the  number of card types purchased during the game. This game is smooth and fun and a thinker.

Pros: Unique interactive card action play, Multiple ways to gain points. Fun resource management elements.

Cons: no gripping art or design, unnecessarily large box to store, does not scale as well for low player counts (easier)

RATING: 8
This is a solid, fun design.  It's true that you are likely to take several actions which help your opponents over the course of the game, and that you'll likewise benefit from what happens on your opponents' turns, sometimes, though admittedly this didn't happen as often as I expected it would after hearing some reviews of the game.  Generally, it is like some other games in that you know your opponents can benefit from many of your potential actions, and so you seek out the action that has a significant benefit to you while avoiding helping your opponents too much.  I enjoy games wherein each turn involves playing an action card, and this game expands on that core mechanic by allowing you to add more cards, and I love the victory mechanism based on deck composition and its relation to several performance metrics.  I look forward to playing the game more.

RATING: 7
This has a Navegador scoring system with cards written all over it. Lovely wooden bits. The game is screaming for an expansion with a better set of purchasable cards.

The card mechanics and whatnot are interesting enough, but for all the effort that went into the historical ins and outs of the various regions the game never had that much of a Roman feel to it. That's a pity because it feels like more variation within the purchasable cards could have easily added some proper historical feel to the setting. Instead each card is just a production or a minor variation to the starting cards. As purchasable cards reveal themselves you more often than not find yourself buying them for the victory point multiplier, with little thought to the action the card provides. The card mechanic thus finds itself taken over by the points scoring system, which engineers a tight game, but not one that lives up to its potential.

There's a few decent choices to be made on your turn, but they're relatively quick choices between 1-3 cards, so the pace of the game should be quite quick. If it's not then it should be. Turns can usually be planned out well in advance. The game is rather light and shouldn't take much over an hour with experienced players. Happy to play again with experienced players who can keep the pace ticking along.

Stop it, designers! Just stop making awesome games. I can't keep up. And if you keep making such wonderful games, I won't be able to play them all.

RATING: 8
My new favorite Mac Gertz game featuring colonization and commerce in the Holy Roman Empire. Play a card to take an action. Play a Tribune to reclaim your played cards. Each card is worth victory points based on the condition of the board. Do you go for more cards, or build more houses and colonists to make your cards worth more VPs? Hint: Probably both. Hard to visualize what your score is or who's winning until the end of the game, but still an interesting game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game is fantastic. I love the way that the action cards are also end game scoring, and the interplay with building yourself up on the board. I like how there are so many places to compete with the other players, and a ton of interaction without the interaction being negative.

There are competing incentives to build into as many regions as possible so that you can benefit when other players produce and get end game scoring or build up a single region so that it is efficient for you to produce there. 

While I have heard concerns about the Concordia card, I think it works well. It rewards rushing to build houses, and forces players to be a little more thoughtful about reducing the number of cards in the offer to below 3. I've won with and without it.

The expansion maps change things up, but don't fundamentally alter the game.

Salsa adds forum tiles that give you special abilities that can push you in different directions and make you value certain actions differently, which makes the game more strategically diverse. It also adds salt, which is a wildcard resource. I think both are great additions, but the implementation of salt city scoring can be problematic. Salt cities can be scored for one Minerva card, which allows the Weaver and to a lesser extent the Vintner to become incredibly efficient. Scoring 30 points off of the Weaver is pretty common when playing with salt, and I can't help thinking that salt cities should be slightly cheaper and not score for Minerva cards at all.

RATING: 1
Spread Sheet Victory Point Matrix the Game. 

I really wonder what happened to Gerdts. Imperial is a true classics, the new ones were just euro puzzles with a rondel. And now he is here - same thing with no rondel. More or less MPS game with some leeching (yay!) and "soft blocking" (you have to pay more!). The key is building your deck of actions - it works kinda similar to Havana (having a special action to take discard back to your hand).  

Graphics are hard to read and pretty generic. The game is about optimising your way through the spread sheet with some luck and randomness included as is in fashion these days. Both Brügge and Ginkopolis are better versions of card play with the board.

Rating down to 1.0 because: uninspired AND themeless AND tedious looking (beige overkill). Too pedestrian.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Played once with the Salsa expansion with 5P. Concordia further refines the concept where players play through a personal deck, and are required to refresh their deck every now and then. The Diplomat, which allows players to copy an opponent's card, adds an interesting tweak to player dynamics.

The mechanisms are well integrated here. Players expand production in a Power Grid-esque format, generating resources for cash and cards, which in turn assist in expanding production further. Cards also serve as endgame multipliers, so players need to know when to begin pushing for endgame scoring.

I do have an issue with the cardplay; it seems fairly important to keep track of what opponents have played and have in their hand. This for the purpose of optimising the use of the Diplomat and to know what opponents' are gunning for in endgame scoring, among other things. But this is fairly difficult to do in a 5P game which lasts potentially over 2 hours.

RATING: 7.9
Played a game, and bought it.

I'm usually not much into area control, but I rather like this game. There is no single strategy or type of resource that is absolutely necessary to win the game; The best strategy appears to often be one that nobody else is going for.

I think we underrated the importance of buying extra hand cards, which are at the same time the only source of converting stuff on the board into victory points -- not to mention that they allow you to do more actions before having to spend an action on getting the played cards back.

What of course is always a big plus for a game is that there is very little randomness.

In short, I'm rather fond of this game, and it absolutely lived up to the expectations in the second round that we played back at home.

RATING: 9
As someone who respects Euros, but does not necessarily love them, know that Concordia is still my all-time favorite of the genre. Brilliant design with complex strategy and player interaction that is more interesting than most other Euros (especially older ones). 

If you are a fan of the genre, and honestly even if you aren't, given Concordia a try! It takes a special game to stand out in the sea of Euros that exists in 2018, but this game does, and will remain an all-time classic alongside Puerto Rico, Agricola, and other more modern stars.

Go watch the Shut Up and Sit Down review for more information about this brilliant game, and learn why it gets mentioned in several of their other game reviews.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
1 play, 3er

This is an economic, card-management game, this time without a rondel! Card handling is sort of like Rococo: everyone starts with the same mix, but can acquire more by buying from a card row. Here, though, you can pick up your discard pile any time, by using the right action card. Cards allow you to do different actions, like moving pieces on the board, building settlements, collecting resources, etc. 

There's an interesting type of timing thing: you want to be able to work with the pace others are, to take advantage of producing at the right time so you can sell at the right time. You also want to delay taking the income action, so you get more money; but don't wait so long that someone else gets it! You want to expand early, to claim key territory; but the longer you wait (to collect the necessary resources), the more you'll be able to expand at once. 

Also interesting is the end-game VP awards, which are based on the cards you've collected during the game. Cards are associated with one of the gods; each god scores VP a different way, with each card being a multiplier, e.g. (# cards) x (# brick settlements you have). So you're balancing end-game VP awards with in-game card action powers.

Cool game, although maybe not the best with 3: too much board space.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
2-5, 100m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This was my first Mac Gerdts game.  From what I know of him the rondel is his bread and butter and this is one of only a couple games without a rondel.  Instead this is a trading game predicated on deck-building and action card selection.

The game starts with each player controlling a sea and land colonist on a map of southern Europe/northern Africa and a hand of the same action cards.  Each turn is simply a player playing one of these action cards.  However there are a lot of decisions and a lot of strategy behind those action cards.

The actions you can take with these cards include moving your colonists and establishing new colonies.  Generating goods which are used to build colonies, colonists, and buy more action cards for your deck.  Buying and selling goods.  And buying new cards for your deck.  The last two cards are the neatest ones.  One of them copies the last played card of your opponent, the other takes back all your cards so you can play them again.  Knowing when to play these two cards are a couple of the most interesting decisions in the game.

The scoring mechanic in this game is a really neat and original one.  Each of the cards in your deck, both starting and bought throughout the game, have a Roman god's name printed on them.  Each god scores points at game end for different things like amount of money, number of colonists, etc. and you score those points a number of times equal to the number of cards for that god you have.

I will say that I don't think this game is that fantastic with just two.  With just two we tended to split off and work just in our own corners of the map with very little interaction.  This devolved the game into a very solitaire experience where we were really only competing for the new action cards and otherwise just did our own thing and compared scores at the end.

There also isn't a huge variety of different cards so I am afraid even with more players and more interaction you'll have "been there, done that" after a number of plays and it will just start to feel same-y and kind of uninteresting.  It's still a very clever and interesting game, I just don't think it is going to withstand a lot of play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Interesting hand management euro game. The theme is over done, but the mechanics is what made this game shine! I really liked the card play in this game. Each card has an  one of the game's actions on it, and then there are two additional cards, one for copying another player's card and one to pick up your discard pile. You could buy additional cards to expand your hand. These cards where similar to the cards you already had (sometimes more powerful), but since each card is a multiplier for end game scoring, it made for interesting decisions. I did find it sometimes difficult to figure out how well I was doing, but maybe I just need to play it more. Also I found that this game scales well thanks to the two-sided board.

RATING: 6
The ugliest box cover to don a top 100 geek game? Because it will make top 100' possibly top 20. A natural and lovely progression from  the rondel to a quasi deck builder. The scoring feels like Navegador but integrates into actions selections as every card provides points as well as actions. It's not quite a deck builder as you don't havea random card draw - you re always in control of what cards you have to play. Simple rules but loads of options - ultimately tricking a balance between the engine and the VP. Need to explore more and I expect familiarity will led to more focused strategies and a little bit of counter drafting. One of the best games I have played from Essen 13 and proof tht there is still life in the middle weight Euro

The card action mechanic is quite like using the rondel at the start of the game as you can not select the same action till the cards come around again. Where it changes (and becomes more interesting) is in purchasing new actions. As its not really a deck builder in the random draw from a deck is not there is no no down side (other than opportunity cot of resources and actions) to filling your deck.
 

2nd play not so keen. A disguised point salad? With a lot of min max. Poor heuristics. V difficult to see how others are doing. 


3rd play heuristics better and enjoyed more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Each turn a player uses an action cards, which also functions as a Navegador-like scoring multiplier -- e.g., Architect cards allow building of cities; each such card in a player's deck at the end of the game is worth 1 VP per non-brick city. Other actions include producing goods in regions (1VP per region a player has a presence in), buying/selling goods (2VP per type of goods produced), acquiring colonists (2VP per colonist), purchasing new cards etc. 

It works well enough -- expand, buy cards, balance acquisition of scoring multipliers with doing of actual stuff. If not quite multiplayer solitaire, it does manage to be even less interactive than Navegador (the left-right binding issue in that game handily resolved by making you not care what anyone else is doing at all!). The card system is so under-utilized -- it needs an expansion with some more interesting cards! -- that it hardly seems like an improvement from the rondel.

Smooth but bland. Great components and presentation other than the hideous box cover.

RATING: 9
I played this 5 times (with all different player numbers) already and enjoyed it every time. The signature of the designer for me is not the action wheel, but the fact that there's some way your actions are offered, without being too restricting. In that way he brings a quick flow to a game, without losing the strategic appeal, on the contrary. I have tried some different paths to victory and it stays interesting. All actions have their strength, all actions feel rewarding (almost in a "settlers-way"), interaction is there and the scoring system is an act of genius. 
When I can add to this that the (clearly presented) rules only take 4 pages, I can conclude with "top game".

RATING: 10
Concordia has a simple ruleset that leads to a nice amount of think during playing.  The single card action keeps you from going into total AP mode.  Sometimes I smack myself for not maximizing each move but, hey, it is what it is.  The length is good (at least with 2 players) and the components are classic Euro.  I like the art and graphic design.  It reads well and looks like an old boring map.  I dig that.  

I feel that the battle for Saturn is crucial in the two player.  It's been the decider in almost all of our games. 

Fantastic replay ability. 

You know, it kind of reminds me of China a bit.  Simple, sparse and very re-playable.  Except this one plays well with 2.   I'm very excited about the release of the Britannia map.

update:  This game has passed the test for 2 player gaming goodness.  There's enough going on that it doesn't feel like a race.  I have been able to compete with Saturn if my opponent goes for that.

Also expansion:
Britannia - Excellent addition for the 2 player space.
Salsa - Very god expansion and probably the best 2 player map.

RATING: 9.4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I haven't played all of Mac Gerdts games, but all of the ones I have played I have very much enjoyed. So this is a game that I was very much looking forward to trying. It having only 4 pages of rules was quite a surprise. And 2 of those 4 pages are the explanations of what the cards do. In fact, the rules go like this: on your turn play one card and do what it says. That's it. Of course the devil is in the details. How do the actions on the cards interact with each other? What strategies emerge from those actions? And what emerges is a very strategic resource gathering, city building, hand building (not really deck building, because you get to choose from your whole deck) game that is so simple in rules yet quite complex to figure out how to play. Especially the first few turns. All of us just tried some stuff. One player started buying cards only to figure out she needed the resources to build cities to get more resources. She recovered though and scored well (but not well enough, haha!). After about 75% of the game I was comfortable enough with the way the game played to start worrying about the score. So I took a look at my deck and figured out what I could do maximize my score, starting from where I was at that point.
I was very pleasantly surprised by the depth and different strategies the game offers with such simple rules. The fact that I won, might have helped, but while I had a good feeling about the outcome, I thought some other people where in the running as well, yet it felt very good. Looking forward to the next session!

Initial rate: 7.5

RATING: 5

RATING: 6
Really lovely game with echoes of [thing=19600]Antike[/thing] minus the direct conflict ([thing=66589]Navegador[/thing] is probably the better comparison, but whatever). Concordia is streamlined and tense. It neatly converts the rondel mechanic into a deck-building style mechanic, and asks the players when they'd like to reshuffle their deck. If you wait, you get a bigger bonus, but sacrifice some of the actions you really want to take. If you reshuffle early, you get the cards you want, but forgo a serious bonus. Combine that with the feeling of expanding your influence over the board and you get a game that I can enjoy!

My big (but only) complaint is the scoring system, which is frustratingly opaque, especially for new players. Buying cards gets you special abilities, but also scores you points -- sort of. The cards actually multiply the points you earn, but do so in a way that feels less obvious than it could be. What I want is a system that forces me to choose between cards that allow me to do a lot in the game, but potentially score less points, and cards that score a lot of points, but do less during the game. There is some of that in Concordia, but also a lot of overlap that muddies the decision space. For experienced players, this is no problem, but for everyone else (including the casual player), the learning curve is steep. Unfortunately, I'm not sure the learning curve is worth the effort. Time will tell. The game is enjoyable, and I look forward to playing more. I only wish I could play more than occasionally. As is, this same complaint keeps rearing its head, which pushes my rating down slightly.

The truth is I enjoy playing Concordia, but when it comes time to score, I feel as though the rug has been pulled out from underneath me. The big reveal at the end of the game always makes me feel as though someone was withholding important information (or like I've been tricked). On a similar note, I wish the game had better player interaction. Taking cards and placing cities others may want gives the impression of direct conflict, but it's really not. It's just too friendly! 

I did have to laugh about the theme. It's literally just trading in the Mediterranean -- difficult to get more cliched than that. Granted, this is actually a positive for me (I love trade-route games!), but don't expect a game dripping with thematic nuance.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Concordia has gotten a lot of buzz on BGG, so I was happy to get a chance to try it.  Concordia is all about card play and it's done in a way I haven't seen before.  Everyone starts with a hand of cards, then you take turns playing a card from your hand to do an action.  The cards also serve as victory points, with each card providing points for different criteria.  Later on you buy more cards which shape your strategy by determining both your available actions and your criteria for victory points.

The card play was very cool, although I didn't feel like I was close to grasping the strategy.  Beyond that, though, you are just trading goods for money and/or VPs with relatively low interaction.  Downtime could be a problem with higher player counts, but it didn't bother me much at 4 players because I was always thinking about my move.  As themeless euros go, this is a good game and I can understand the hype.  But I wouldn't be surprised if I never played it again.

RATING: 7
A game with a deckbuilding element that has (almost) perfect information. What I mean is that you get the cards you aquire in the course of the game directly into your hand and once you choose a specific action you get all cards played back into your deck to chose from. So there's no shuffling and hoping to draw the right card or the right combo of cards. Also, there's quite a nice twist since every card purchased for your "deck" will be a part of your victory point engine in the end. 

----------------------------------
Slightly downgrading it since it is really hard to keep track of how good or bad your opponents are doing. Most of the time people are focussed on their "puzzle" without any interaction taking place. At least no interaction that could be credited to the games mechanics. I still like the different deckbuilding approach and the nice touch of the final scoring according to how you build your deck with every card of your deck providing victory points.

RATING: 10
Excellent game. There is almost no conflict, but there is definite interaction in the form of competition for drafted cards, building in cities, and a unique production mechanic where you make entire areas produce goods, so you often end up giving resources to your opponents while getting things for yourself. Timing is also very important because you want to take actions in the right order, but you're also usually waiting for your opponents to play the right cards so you can copy it.

Update: Oh the expansion. The expansion is perfect in every way and pushed this from a 9 to a 10. My only complaint about the game was that it can be a little too hard to do what you want to do sometimes (for example, it can take several turns to get the right resources because of the way the different actions work). The expansion helps make things [i]slightly[/i] easier while still maintaining a tight game.

The first thing is the salt itself. It counts as a wild good, and everybody starts with one salt, so it gives you much more flexibility early on. After that, it can be pretty tough to get salt so you have to use it wisely.

The next thing is the forum tiles. These can give you a small boost throughout the game (like gaining extra money every time you sell goods) or they can be one-time use tiles that can help you take a super-turn.

The last thing is the new board, which is double-sided. If you didn't buy the map-only expansion, then this is a good way to get more variety outside of the base game boards.

So we have two new things (salt and forum tiles) that add very little complexity to the game but help give it an overall better feel, plus new boards just for variety. Excellent expansion.

[b]Player Count Verdict: 3-4[/b]
With 2 players there isn't enough competition (though I haven't tried the smallest map, which appears to be specifically for 2 players) and the diplomat cards become really restrictive. Although the game plays fine with 5 players, there can be quite a bit of downtime. With 3-4 players you get a good mix of competition, useful diplomat actions, plus downtime isn't too bad.

RATING: 7.7
Solid, medium-weight euro with what feels like a large number of meaningful decisions and viable strategies. The variability in board setup forces you to re-evaluate your strategy every game. It doesn't feel like there's any one "best" strategy. Player interactions are plentiful as well, giving the game a tactical feel without feeling frustrating. Getting blocked outright rarely happens, limiting the number of meaningless turns you'll experience in the game.
The mechanics are well designed, elegant, and finely tuned, however, I found myself wishing for a for maybe just a few more unique personality cards. Towards the end, I found myself just buying cards to increase multipliers, not because I actually wanted the actions. This isn't necessarily a bad thing, and the cards' dual purpose is clever and elegant, but the actions (especially the "advanced" actions that you buy) are maybe just a bit too direct. 

Overall, I feel like it's a very solid game. I'd rank it just above most other euros of its weight class (Istanbul, Five Tribes, etc). If you're looking for something that's easy to teach, flows well, and feels satisfying to play I'd definitely recommend it. However, it's not going to replace heavier games with deep, fiddly puzzles like Caverna/Agricola, or A Feast For Odin, Terra Mystica, etc.

RATING: 9.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Concordia is a deckbuilder where you never have to thin your deck, that's also a boardgame.  Every turn you play a card to take an action.  Any cards you haven't yet played are available to you, and whenever you want to retrieve your played cards, there's a card that let's you do that.  Additionally, every card potentially contributes to your score, as all cards have some sort of victory point condition.  If you meet the condition, you get the points on the card.  If you have five cards of the same type, you get the points for every card.  

You're racing your "colonists" around the board to build trading houses in different cities in various provinces.  Each city produces a different good.  Your trading houses can provide the appropriate good when that province is triggered.  Goods help you build trading houses and colonists.  And there is a card row where you can buy new cards from, the newer cards cost more.  At the end of the game you get points from money (marginally), and depending on what cards you have, colonists and trading houses in a) different cities, b) different provinces, c) different types of goods, d) one of the five specific types of good.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[thing=124361][/thing] is a Mac Gerdts' game, the lord of the rondel, whose fame comes from titles like [thing=66589][/thing] and [thing=24181][/thing]. In the game, each player represents a family, a dinasty of romans that expands the borders of the Empire. Each player starts with the same deck of cards, and each of these has a different use - the Merchant allows to gain money and trade goods for other goods; the Prefect activates one region and it produces the main product there for the player that used the action, and also each building built there also produce, but then to the players that own them - the player can also use the Prefect to reactivate all the regions there are in the activated state, gaining money as indicated in them; the Architect allows the players to move ships and colonist, and also to build buildings ins places connected with them; the Diplomat allows the player to copy the last card used by another player; and there are other cards with other functions.

In her turn the player picks one of the cards to use, and put it on the table, and the card stays there. The player do the action allowed by the card, and done, next player turn. Through the cards all the actions in the card, even buy more cards.

Because on the board, besides regions and type of buildings, there is also a row of cards: the ones closer to the front of the row are cheaper to buy, the further it is, the costier it is to buy them, and this is coupled with the unique cost that each card has - so the player pays this unique cost plus the cost accordingly to the place in which the card is. Once bought, the card or cards come into the player hand, and these new cards mostly do the same action the starting ones do, some just a little better, only a few (I think only the Minerva ones) do something completely new, which is activate all the types you have of one type of building (the one connected with the card). Mostly these cards, as actions go, will only allow to do more of something you already could do. This is important, because, since the cards used stay in the board, you can only do the same action again (except the Prefect, since you start with 2 cards of it), once you use the Tribune, to collect all the cards back into your hands. So, with more cards, you can use more actions before using the Tribune (and the Tribunes pays more, since you gain 1 money for every card above three you collect).

And, besides this, not only is important what the card to as action, but also (or even mainly), to which god it is connected, since this will translate into points in the game end. So, the more cards you have, more points. And the more you have of one type of god, more points you will receive in the area the god punctuate. So let's say Mercury give 2 points for every different type of building you have. If you have 4 different types by the game end, every Mercury card will net you 8 points. So, if you have 4 of them, you will gain 32 points (4 x 2 x 4). And this apply to every god. Therefore, not only you have to prepare well in the board (expanding, building, collecting resources), but also you must be aware of the cards there are in the row, trying to not let some great action/scoring card pass you by. And this creates a fine tension, since you mostly wants to leave the cards drops in cost, to pay less for them, but this is somewhat dangerous, since the player to your left can buy 2, the next one 1 or 2, and the cards in your turn were costly, for the third player, are now cheap - will she get them? Shouldn't you just buy the cards, even if overpaying? Tough to say.

Well, [thing=124361][/thing] is a medium level complex game, not too heavy nor overly simple, giving the players a series of options each turn, usually with several of them being good, but you always vying for the best one, since there is a component of timming to the actions being made - like, if you want a card the demands wine to be paid, and no player has wine, and you do, you know you can take your time - any other player will first have to use an action to acquire wine, and since only one action can be made each turn, if one player do this, in your turn, you can buy the card (however, this go both ways: if you want a card, and don't have wine, and someone else has, you might be one step behind, and must hope the other player don't want, didn't notice the card or simply has a better action to do in that moment.

[thing=124361][/thing] is a pretty interesting game, but play smart: there is no catch up mechanism, nor even bash the leader type of action (there is no combat, the best players can do is build first, buy cards the other wants or avoid activating a region where other players have buildings). In my first play, in my very first turn, I bought 2 cards, and, without resources to build, I lagged behind the whole game! Be careful while spending.

RATING: 7
Initial impressions: Concordia is beloved by many. You'd expect a game like Concordia to have lot of fiddly bits and many obscure rules. Yet, I believe the game is a hit primarily because it is simple to teach but has a fair amount of depth. On each turn, players play a card and do the actions on each card. The actions are relatively straight forward and even as you increase the size of the deck, most of the actions are variants of the original set of 7. The game does have a point-salad like quality as there are many areas for scoring and a score pad is required to tabulate the scores. While it feels like there are unique paths to victory, specialization is probably helpful but diversification is just as important as I don't think one route of scoring is sufficient. 

The game does have lots of "conflict" and player interaction is quite high as players jostle to position their houses in cities. This can lead to lots of angst and player frustration as resources are ever so limited.  So while turns in Concordia are relatively quick, it can get bogged down occasionally with AP as there are a fair amount of things to do and you fall in to the "there are 10 things you want to do but not enough rounds" dilemma. 

If there is one criticism of Concordia, it is that the game hinges entirely on accumulating scoring cards for end game scoring. All elements on the board are scored based on the type of cards collected for scoring. There is a balance between collecting scoring cards and advancing your position on the board. At some point, there is a switch in the game as players rush to collect score cards. The switch can be abrupt and the end game can be unexpected. More importantly, this can be frustrating for some players who enjoy tableau building and engine building where 
there is less conflict. Imagine your best plans to score laid to waste when the score cards you want are quickly scooped up by others, sometimes inadvertently because it just happens to be available for other to score a couple of points. That can be vexing. 

Overall, this is a good game and perhaps even a great game. I consider this one of Mac Gerdts's best design  alongside Princes of Macchu Picchu. PErhaps the rondel is just not for me as much as both these designs do not have a giant wheel to move around.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

On it's own, it's fine, but the expansion maps and Salsa really help lengthen the design.

It moves briskly and is interesting... but it's hard to get too excited about a game of changing resources into resources into money into more resources.

RATING: 8
It's nice every now and again to run into a new game that is actually a throw back to the classic Euros. Sure, there are some decidedly non-Euro things here such as deckbuilding, hand management, etc. But even when you are navigating those things, the payoff is route building, dropping little wooden houses and claiming majorities. The feel is pure Euro.

I was hoping that deckbuilding would evolve away from being it's own genre and into being just another tool in the shed. I'm glad Martin Wallace is no longer the only one trying. Sure, this is actually just the rondel mechanism moved to cards...but that makes it so much more flexible while at the same time completely eliminating the need for rules about what action you can take and how much it costs you. The cards do all the work and let you focus on the map, so you can make the right decisions about which way to spread your influence.

This is one of my favorite things to come out of 2013.

RATING: 7
I've spoken at length about why ultimately Concordia failed to endure in my collection everywhere else but here, but now that I've sold it, it's time to give it the post-mortem.

Concordia is still a fine game given its directness and transparency of action, and as long as it's taught correctly, it's scoring dynamic (mid-game scoring is ESSENTIAL when teaching new-comers. DON'T OMIT IT).

Ultimately my problem with Concordia is its lack of escalation and change over time, and this combined with its play-length that even with 3 players begins to wear. Where Navegador has a player-driven, interactive market and dynamics that play off one another, players more or less enact Concordia on their own. The three points of inter-player dynamic being the goods generation/taxation cycle, the escalating costs of trade-houses in single cities and the diplomat card are all good, but not good enough. The diplomat would be the most interesting dynamic given it's a timing bonus to those who use it wisely, and while the taxation dynamic is also fairly solid, it's ultimately less interesting than the dynamic market of Navegador. It also favour one player only and ultimately rather than having a player only able to manipulate the index of one of three goods at a time. By direct comparison, the market/trade action in Concordia is empty of intrigue and is rendered a labour action rather than an interesting one, and the semi-random draw of goods distribution will largely determine the spatial strats of players.

Even though a player may launch all of their colonists, essentially giving them more movement ability in the late-game, this isn't really a dynamic game-changer or terribly meaningful, nor is the extra warehouse space it affords. Concordia's greatest strength - the simplicity of playing one card and executing its action, is also its weakest, as after so much labour, players still enact actions piecemeal, one tiny component at a time. I'm a fan of engines, combos and super-moves, things you earn by the increasing mastery of the systems at hand, but Concordia suffers a pacing problem once the mid-game begins as there is absolutely no escalation.

It also feels as though there aren't any distinct strats, either - everyone is doing the same thing, just in mildly differing ways ie. regions and by what goods -  but everyone is still establishing trade-houses in cities by which they'll score according to deck criteria. Navegador by contrast has the exploration strat, production strats, refining strats, the church, and combinations of those depending on how players want to bias their approach.

It's no surprise that Concordia is Gerdts' most popular title with new-school Eurogamers, and I'll have no problem finding a copy to play in my circles should I ever feel the need, but now that I regard play-length in particular to be a crucial point, in the end, the game doesn't offer enough in the time it has. This is no knife-fight in a phone-booth, and I'm fully aware it's not meant to be. Nevertheless, I'm not terribly fond of trying to throw stones at a thousand miles.

RATING: 8.5
Surely a good game, most of all due to the millions of slightly different strategies you can follow to rake points out of retty much everything in the board. You decide which of these ways you will follow by taking cards that allow you also more actions, that's great. The best idea is the card that allows you to copy the action of other players: it makes for some very very interesting decisions.
On the other hand, the game is very very slow to start (maybe this is just the problem of the first game) and it is utterly unreadable: you don't know (unless you spend hours counting) how many points you have or the others have. While in Antike you can see everything at a glance, here you just can't know who's winning. I won my first game and I thought i was last!
The game seems very balanced and it seems everyone is reaching the same point after walking different paths. There's no direct interaction and the indirect interaction isn't very important, with the exception of the cards and the diplomat action.
My rating would be a 7.8 but i give it an 8 so far because i am sure i'll appreciate it more after my second game.

I love this feeling Gerds' games give me toward 3/4 of a game, when I am like "gee, this is really a well working game!" Anyways, nothing compares to Antike so far!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The guys on The Secret Cabal had a lot of great things to say about Concordia. A lot. Jumping out of their pants a lot. I love their podcast, but I also know that they can get really excited about new games and they all have a lot of “favorites”. Yet, their review of Concordia drew me buying it, closing out yet another CoolStuffInc order.

Cut forward to TableTop Day and a lot of my friends were requesting this one which I had described as “literally trading in the Mediterranean”. Somehow I managed to wrangle up four other players who thought that sounded good. Well, I have to agree with The Secret Cabal that it is a good game. Like other Mac Gerdts games it has short turns with simple actions which should decrease the amount of analysis paralysis. It has a variable setup and a double sided board (along with another double-sided expansion board) to give you some variability.

Then there’s what makes the game different from Mac Gerdts’s other games: the card play. Instead of using a rondel for action selection like Mac’s other games, you have a hand of cards all with different actions on them: move and build, produce resources in a province, buy new cards, trade goods with the bank, and a reset that allows you to pick up all of your cards. Moving from a rondel to the cards does slow things down a bit, especially when you have a hand full of juicy cards. Yet, as you play you start to understand exactly what it is you’re trying to do and there’s not going to be much that other players can do to stop you from your plans (with the exception of producing goods in the province you were about to use or buying that card you wanted).

I overall really liked this game, the trading works well, the building of a network is fun to watch as it expands across the map, more colonists mean you can expand faster and further, and specialization and diversification are both valid strategies.

My only concern with this game is play time and I’ve only played the game at it’s fullest player count. With five people I think it took us two and a half hours which is about an hour longer than I want it to be. I’ll have to try the game with fewer people and see if I like it nearly as much at the lower player counts.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.9
Well designed game that has very little "luck factor".   Significant amount of strategy and pre-planning required to sequence an efficient spread across the map.   Good replayability.  The new maps and expansion averts what might otherwise become a little repetitive if exclusively playing a two player game.  Scales well, smaller board provides greater tension for two person games.  A five person game gets a little tight in terms of money, which adds further decision making as to how to balance expansion with production and income.  One of the more well rounded games out there!  

It's rare to find a game that provides a feeling of progression, advancement and achievement -all within about 90 minutes (four player game).  Concordia does this well!

RATING: N/A
Travel the provinces of the Mediterranean and build houses in regions rich in farming, wine, cloth, or brick. Cities in the region start with a random resource. Each player has the same starting deck of action cards with basic actions but also some clever twists. The senator allows a deck building action but when is the right time to buy that card? So many strategy paths to consider; control which set of provinces or resources, or building types, which pantheon fits best with your plans, and which Gods? Even the resource market works very well with the other systems.  Such a slick game. One of my favorites. But, I can't get my game group (or wife) as excited about playing it. Sigh.

UPDATE: If I ever get this game, Salsa is a must get expansion.

RATING: 8
I was quite intrigued when I saw this game for the first time. It took us a while to understand all the mechanics.

After understanding the rules and the mechanics, this is a very fun game and there are more than one way to win.

Some people say this game is very solitaire and that you can plan your moves while your opponents have their turn. I don't agree.

When you try to get the 'bonus' province commodity, you will think about the other player to try to get commodities for yourself, while minimising resources for other players. Also, there is the 'Diplomat' card that allows you to mimic another player's last played card. You may think you have a plan of action, but someone may play a card that gives you a better payoff than your planned move.

The pieces and the board itself is beautifully crafted and the game mechanic is very interesting.

RATING: 6
Conceptually it's a very easy asynchronous action selection, area control game.

The cards you purchase throughout the game are bought primarily to give you benefits as you play rather than for whatever end game scoring they provide.  You'll also likely be unable to track which card types your opponents have purchased throughout the game.  This means the ending score is somewhat of a surprise as you look through what cards you ended up with and hope your opponents don't have better cards than you.  The focus of the game also shifts in the endgame once the board has been fully colonized.  It's almost as if the whole premise and theme of the game no longer matters and all you want to do is buy cards to score more points. 

What little theme the game has is completely bland.  It's mostly just a hodgepodge of mechanics that surprisingly work very well together, but ultimately you'll feel like you're just buying resources in order to buy cities and cards in order to harvest more resources, and repeat.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.75
I have played this game a hand full of times and very much enjoy it. It is both deceptively easy to pick up and play, yet it has a ton of depth and strategy while still being a lot of fun.

The game is a step up from the typical "gateway" game but not by much. It combines a lot of simple mechanics in to something that's quite different from anything else that I own.

I like how the cards not only act as your means to perform actions but that once you have used a card you are unable to use that card (action) again until you take a turn to reset your hand. Further, they act as a means to generate points at the end of the game based on the type of card that they are.

This duality of the cards is great as you need to buy more cards throughout the game in order to acquire end game points but at the same time you're going to want certain cards because they allow you to perform new or better actions than the actions that you started out with. The ability to copy someone else's card and gain benefits (goods) from other peoples actions just adds another level of interest to this game and makes you engaged whether or not it's your turn.

And while some complain that you can't really tell who's winning because the scoring occurs all at once at the end of the game, I think that you're generally able to tell who is doing well based on how things are distributed on the board and by seeing the types of cards that they are buying.

At the end of the day, this a great game that has tons of replayability due to the variability in the set-up, maps, cards and strategies of the people that you're playing with.

RATING: 9.5
I was invited by a friend to a BGGCon and this game really surprised me! It is by far my favorite game I own or have played. It's a Euro-style game that relies both on the board and deck building for actions. You are a Roman merchant influencing the ancient roman empire. While there is conflict with other players it's more about out-maneuvering others than it is actually directly attacking other players. It plays well with multiple maps with the expansion! Make sure to expand your card deck hand! For new players the mock scoring round is key. This game takes about 1 to 2 hours.

RATING: 8.5
For most complex, deep euros there's one thing that annoys me, something that is surprisingly counterintuitive or inelegant.  For Terra Mystica it's the way your adjacent buildings plays differently for end game scoring or building a city.  For Caylus it's the ladder to the blue buildings.  

Concordia didn't have one.  I'm impressed.

RATING: 7.5
Lots of goodness in here. Simple rules, [thing=15600]Kreta[/thing]-style deck cycling, cards that you need to buy for their functionality, but they also define what scores you victory points, so you want to try to optimize that, plenty of hand management and [the good kind] of opportunistic play. It even plays well 2p!

Oh! Also, a solid A for graphic design. The only thing keeping this graphical marvel from an A+ is the lack of a "×⌂" on or beside the sestertii (money) portion of the building cost. Even the setup is depicted on the backs of the player boards. Fantastic!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
7/9/2016
Played it with 3 players and it's very good. It went long however because there is a setup rule which i had been doing wrong from the beginning. I never removed cards according to number of players. The last two times it was not an issue as i played with 5.

It's an easy going euro game with limited interaction between players. What i love is how simple and quick your turn usually is. Play a card and do what the card says. Even though the interaction is limited i find myself interested at what other people are doing for the following reasons:

1. Whether to use the diplomat or not
2. Who can get cards this turn (i.e. what goods they have)
3. Who can build (again what goods they have)
4. Movement on the board

The only issue is the scoring which for first timers is very obscure until the very end. I tried the intermediate scoring but it adds a lot of time and doesn't really help i think. I will try and do some more examples during rules explanation.

It's a game i would introduce to almost anyone due to it being light on rules even though the strategy might be more demanding.

Update 15/9/2016
Finally played it with 4 and it's definitely the sweet spot for the Med map. This game gets better with every play. This time, i was playing with 2 new players and Greg and i decided to try things a bit differently. I played the Tribune very early on and tried to build a colonist every time. My focus was on Jupiter, Saturn, Mars and the wine Minerva card. Saturn was not worth it as i realised late on that i only had 2 cards. 

This is a very enjoyable game and very easy for people to play though difficult to understand the scoring until after 1-2 plays.
